RATING: 10
Extremely limited action game with an integral auction component and multiple (?) paths to victory.

The game lasts seven turns, with each turn consisting of an auction phase, where each player purchases one item, and then a limited action phase, where each player performs 2 actions- purchase more useful items and/or "show your work", whereby points and/or money is generated.

The auction phase is interactive and has very subtle strategies to it.  Do you choose an item you're not really interested in, hoping someone else is, and will then purchase themselves out of the auction, leaving less people to compete with you on the item(s) you really want?  Do you get into a bidding war on an item you don't really want in order to make someone else pay more for it?

The action phase, while non-interactive, provides difficult decisions as money is very tight, and there always several things you want to do (purchase).  Also, you have to balance present financial considerations versus future point value of your profession cards (works).  Because of the tight money situation, you will often find yourself showing a work, even though you know you can get more value for it if you held onto to it longer.

There are several options/ways to score and win this game, although I think that some methods are much more dominant than others.  You can acquire jesters, lots of profession cards, and some recruiting cards and then show all of your works towards the end of the game for maximum value.  You can obtain several builders, make alot of buildings and show works early and often to obtain the 3 bonus points for the best work each time.  You can acquire valuable prestige cards and have a mix of profession cards, and use bonus cards to increase their value.  

I have finally seen the light with this game, it is an outstanding game for 3-5 players (haven't played 2-players yet), and although it can be somewhat scripted at times, it is a strategic masterpiece, that is deceptively deep.

Own the [boardgame=165358]Princess and Muse[/boardgame] mini-expansion from the [boardgame=40688]Treasure Chest[/boardgame] and a nice homemade version of the Builders expansion, and I find now that I'd rather always play with the expansion than without it.  It adds several new pathways to victory, despite adding a bit more length to the gameplay.

RATING: 5
Hmm...there's a a lot going on here and lots to think about.  You can pursue lots of different strategies and come from behind.  I liked those things.  On the other hand, it's a trifle dry, the theme isn't very strong and there's very little inter-action between the players. 
 
It's this last quality that worries me the most.  Games like that just don't seem to provide the crucial spark.  I know a lot of folks think that this is the bees knees and I can, perhaps, understand why they would rate it very highly, but I just don't see that it could ever be a top 10 game...but it is rated that here. It's not got nearly enough sting to have any buzz for me.
 
"Fun-wise" it's kind of average...no wait a minute, it's actually a bit below average for that.  Yes, it can be fun to solve a puzzle like this, but not as much as pitting your wits against someone who's competing for the same space as well as the same resources.  Nor are you going to get gasps of surprise, shock or laughter playing this.

Having said that, I can see why it would be popular amongst those who are hard-core heavy-euro players.  And they are the biggest grouping here, which explains the rank of this game. I'm not in that group of people because I like a bit of everything (not just heavy Euros), and in any case with heavier games I like to feel that I am involved in something mentally AND socially stimulating and this  fulfills only the former criteria.  

Having said all of that, I guess if you are the sort who likes to maximise scores in an analytical way and it doesn't bother you whether playing a game paints almost as much of a solo mindscape as doing a Sidoku puzzle, then you would probably favour this over something like Wallenstein or E and T (2 other games in the top 10).

So, I'd say, this is good and worth playing again, but is not amongst the top ones I'd reach for.

RATING: 8
This one I like!  Many options for strategy.  Lots of choices.  Player interaction via auctions and limited supply.  Very interesting!  Rating may improve with additional plays.  I'm glad I bought this one.

Note:  There's a problem playing this with 5 players.  The obvious opening round move is to snag a Profession card with one of your two actions (draw 5, keep 1).  5 players means 6 of the 21 Profession cards remain.  The 5th player to get a Profession card will draw only 2, keep 1.  Very, very bad compared to 1st, 2nd, or 3rd.  Best played with 4, not 5.

RATING: 6
 where you become a patron of the arts and try and oversee the creation of the greatest works by building theatres, laboratories and also lakes and parks for those silly artists to get inspired by. 

The first part of each turn is an auctioning game. Thing is, while there are several of each item only one player can take each auction item every turn, so you may have to adjust your plan!

The second part of each turn you can choose two further actions - buying buildings, completing works etc.

The time constraint is brutal and forces very efficient play.

Update: Trading as never gets played. This is the Rio grande Alea big boxed sized edition.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I enjoyed PoF because it's a relatively short, compact game that has multiple strategies for scoring points. The game is 7 rounds long, which takes between 1-1.5 hours to play. My favourite element of PoF is that it forces you to adapt depending on the game situation.

In PoF, you are forced to pick a strategy fairly early in the game, plan out several moves ahead, re-evaluate and modify your strategy based on auction results, and react to what other players are doing. There never seems to be enough rounds to do everything you want, and yet you have to figure out a way to make it work. Since you are limited in what you can do in the action phase, you need to ensure that you time your actions properly. 

On the other hand, my sister is not a big fan of the game. She says that it feels like she's trying to catch a runaway train (in this case, the work value minimum goes up each round). She says that you're always running out of time and money, and you can't do everything that you want to do. She'd also doesn't like that you can only perform some actions only once during the action phase (i.e. buying a freedom or profession card). She likes Power Grid better. 

RULE MISHAP: We thought that you could only have 1 action for the round if you selected a freedom or profession - my sister is interested in trying PoF again to see if the game improves. For me, it may make my rating go up! 

UPDATE 1: Now with a few more game titles under my belt and having played PoF with heavy board gamers, I've downgraded my rating. There are just so many other board games I would pick to play over this one now - whether it be a heavier one or a light, short game. UPDATE 2: Not as interested in playing this one, but maybe the reissued, redesigned edition will spark my interested. 2015 UPDATE: hard-to-read card text, boring graphics and theme, restrictive building placement, and dated art puts this one in the trade pile.

RATING: 6
2-5(5)

It's all right. The auction is sort of the game, with players each trying to assess their needs (or a jester! which fulfills anything) and then the rest of the game is just sort of an immense efficiency exercise.

It's extremely tight, with so few actions and so much to do, and I think the components can be well-balanced.

Taking money and completing works early is essential (also for the bonus), and there's a big element in timing your works (I assume one won't be able to complete in every round) and boosting them just so to get the point bonus (a pretty big bonus).

And then, like any game of this sort, players are loath to take a "non-productive" action (in In the Year of the Dragon, this would be buying an ultra-valuable privilege), which here would be getting the Presige cards, say, as that's one of your seven auctions, but those can really determine the winner.

There's a bit here, but it's maybe just a bit calculating and head down over your own tableau for me ... I prefer the designers' El Grande over this. By a wide margin.

---------------------------------------------

The auction is certainly flat for two, as bidding (as in Power Grid) can sometimes be flat with both knowing that you can get something else for face. Meaning that you often have to auction the same things (jesters, I suppose?) in order to prevent your opponent getting them cheaply. Which means that some of the metagame of throwing in something that others want to set off a bidding war (of which you aren't involved) gets pulled out.

Still, with two it was a deep game that had a lot of things going on and with a bunch of (good!) point swings. Many out there aren't fans of secret objectives, and indeed I won by merit of my two prestige cards, but I think they're fine here in that they're auctioned and often you need to get them early (at critical cost!). I think with two the secret objectives might be a bit too easy to pull off, especially late, as there is lighter competition in auctions and such, but it's a good play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
The bad thing about Princes of Florence is that it's very calculable.  The rules booklet even provides an exchange rate for the game's two currencies, florins and prestige.  In addition there is a very low luck element.  There are card draws but you get to draw five from a small deck and keep one, so either you will draw something useful or you shouldn't have been drawing from that deck.  

What rescues this game from perfect planning and low replayability is the auctions, which constantly force you to switch from plan A to plans B,C and D, re-calculating as you go.  I'm not sure how I feel about all this calculation.

PoF is an efficiency game, albeit with a strong element of interaction through the auctions.  It's a classical sort of efficiency game, lean and streamlined compared to  designs like Agricola with bewildering amounts of detail and decoration.  Kramer and Ulrich give you just enough to keep things interesting. I like that.
 
No complaints about the production quality and artwork of my Alea edition. I just wish they hadn't used that awful spidery text.

RATING: 6
I actually like a lot of what goes on in Princes of Florence.  The way that auctions really make you choose is a delightful, if not sometimes painful, situation.  The round limit ensures the game doesn't overstay its welcome.  Action Paralysis is kept pretty low as the number of things you can really study are limited.  Even luck is kept low thanks to any card draw letting you choose from the top 5.  Sure, you might not get what you want, but you can pick the best of what's left, or you can steal a card someone else might really benefit from.

It's not a perfect game - there's certainly some unfortunate situations from getting locked out of things, but overall it tends to be enjoyable.

RATING: 8
Brilliant tight auction game for five experienced players.  Suffers from the problem that the items at auction are difficult to evaluate for new players (i.e. if the experienced player is allowed to buy jesters for cheap, then the game will not be close).  Also suffers from the problem that at certain times the decisions are illusory: one option is clearly superior, but the others remain as traps for the uninitiated.

Caveats aside, with a table of five experienced players, this is simply marvelous.  Perhaps the best of the Alea line.

(N.B. I have not played with any of the 'expansions' included in more recent printings, nor do I have any desire to do so, as I do not see how they can improve the game, but I can see how they could significantly weaken it.)

RATING: 8
the auction style where one is forced to increment only by 100 is thought-provoking, still not sure if it's worse for the game than player-sized bids but i do like that it seems to force people down different paths.  no complaints about the solitaire-style stuff really, as the auction interaction is enough and i think it's ok to have some games out there where direct leader bashing isn't an option.  i do wish that the development did more than add work value, though, and contributed something in the way of effects.  turn order issues seem present, especially with the rules as written in 5P games where player two will get an extra profession card.  i agree with the variants that address that by pulling the leftover card and allowing it to be used for recruiting only, but there are still issues with how many times a person goes first and last in a round as well as earlier players getting better picks out of the profession deck.  i've still become a bit fascinated with the system, though, and look forward to every play.

Nagyon jó iparos munka, de nem korszakos alkotás. Szerintem. A szabályok egyszerűek, de a sok döntési lehetőség miatt agyalós a játék.
Művészeket kell a palotánkba csalni Firenzében, a mecénások korában, és úgy építkezni, hogy minél idillibb környezetben alkothassák meg főművüket, hogy az ezáltal minél értékesebb legyen.
A játék hibája szerintem, hogy 7 kör van, így nincs olyan játékosszám, ami esetén mindenki azonos számú körben kezdő. Ennek fokozása, hogy 2 játékosnál a játék legelején elveheti a kezdőjátékos azt a szabadságjog lapkát, amiből 1 van, így a másik végig nem tud szerezni a játék során (megjegyzem ettől még nem a kezdő nyert esetünkben).
Sok eltérő stratégia is eredményes lehet, a fent említett hiba ellenére is elég kiegyensúlyozottnak tűnik játék! Mindenre nem lehet egyszerre ráhajtani, hisz összesen 21 dolgot (épület, kártya, liget, stb.) szerezhetünk, de összesen több, mint 10 félét. Könnyen elmehet a dolog hosszadalmas optimalizálgatás irányába, szóval meg kell gondolni, kivel játszunk:) Én a presztízskártyát sokkal erősebbnek érzem, mint a bónuszt, bár kockázatosabb. Az átcsábító kártyának nem sok értelmét láttuk,de nyilván azért, mert kettőnknek jutott minden féle épület. 
Nagyon sokat számít, hogy jókor kezdjük átváltani a művekért kapott pénzt győzelmi pontra!
A nyelvfüggő kártyák még németül sem okozhatnak gondot, néhány szót kell csak érteni.
Szívesen játszom vele még, de valószínűleg nem lesz kedvenc.
Még annyit, hogy több  játékossal nagyon korlátos lehet több dolog is, pl. építőmester... Kipróbálnám, milyen mondjuk 4-en.
---
Szerencsére újra előkerült. Most hogy többet is játszottunk egymás után, egy nagyon jól sikerült játéknak érzem, javítottam is egyet a ponton. És ami fontos, hogy 70-80 perc alatt simán lemegy egy 3 fős parti. Ma már az átcsábításnak is látom az értelmét, volt olyan parti, ami ezen dőlt el. Minden játékosszámnál más tűnik szűkösnek. 3-an a szabadságjogok, többen az épületek. 4-5 főnél valószínűleg a bónuszok és az átcsábítások jobban kellenek.

Szinte minden partit más taktikával nyerünk meg. És még csak az alap változatnál tartunk...
---
Nagyon jók vannak a variánsok közt is, és a kieg is dob még a játékon. Elismerem, hogy a játékvégi pontkártyák közt nagy a szórás, van amelyiket szinte mindig mindenki teljesíti, másikat meg szinte soha... De ennyi nekem belefér, mivel 5-ből kell választani.
---
A kieggel egyértelműen többutas a játék. Az alapnál van egy olyan stratégia, amit kicsit túl egyszerűnek és faéknak érzek: az első fázisokban csak mutatványost, esetleg átcsábító kártyát gyűjteni. Ligeteket és építészt hagyni a fenébe. A második fázisokban egy vagy max 2 szabadságjogot, esetleg 1-2 épületet, ha több művésznek is segítenek, de inkább csak körönként 1 újabb személyiség szerzése és alkotás készítése. 5 főnél nyilván szűkös lesz a személyiségek száma, akkor több átcsábító kell. De a lényeg, hogy alkotást kell letenni sokat, mert az sokkal többet hoz mint bármi más... És a mutatványos meg a személykártya mindenképp növeli az alkotás értékét, így egy idő után nem is kell nézni milyen személyt szerzünk, 17 fölé megy az érték (5 mutatványos + 7 személy = 17, és ebben nincs benne az esetleges szabadságjog vagy épület), vagyis a vége felé tetszőleges személy leépíthető így, és darabja hoz 8+ pontot. 3 főnél működik, nyilván több játékosnál nehezebb, de ha vész esetén jobb híján épületet is lehet építeni, vagy bónuszkártyát venni. Ezzel a faékkel ráadásul a körök nagy részében mienk a bónusz 3 pont is. Kiegben és a variánsokban simán vannak olyan stratégiák, amivel ez verhető, és lehet hogy az alapban is, én főleg csak a 3 fősről tudok nyilatkozni.

Ja a kiegnél a pont nem váltható vissza pénzre, ezért ott beállhat a játék. Néhányszor hajszálon múlt, hogy nem szívtuk meg.
---
Mostmár annyira egyértelműnek érezzük a mutatványos faék taktikát, hogy kicsit el is bizonytalanodtam a játékkal kapcsolatban. Jó játék alapvetően, de nem jövünk rá a faék ellenszerére. Valószínűleg 5 főnél nincs ilyen gond, de 3 főnél mindenkinek menni kell a mutatványosra, ne legyen valakinek egyoldalan sok... Nálunk mindig az nyert, akinek a legtöbb mutatványosa volt. Akár 1000+ pénzt is megadtunk egy mutatványosért, simán megtérült. Ez kicsit csalódás, de mentségére szolgál, hogy a kieggel többutas marad a játék. Valószínűleg ha a jövőben előkerül, csak kieggel fogjuk használni, most viszont kicsit félretesszük pihenni. A közös épületes kieget is kipróbáltuk, jó kis színt visz a játékba, de nagy kockázat a másiknak egy épületet ajándékozni, nagyon meg kell kérni az árát. Hibának érezzük, hogy a tábláknak ez az oldala nem tartalmazza az épületek listáját, ami nagyon hasznos az alap oldalon.

RATING: 8
Princes of Florence is a game that sat in my unplayed pile for years, after having received it as a gift almost ten years ago. So it has the double honor of being my first "true" euro, and the king of the unplayed games ever.

In the last few months I begun discovering the great genius of Wolfgang Kramer, and I became eager to try it. Finally the occasion came and... it rocks!

It is an awesome blend of auction, action, and puzzle mechanisms, that plays in a multiplayer-solitaire way, with the player interaction limited to (the very tight) auctions.

Princes of Florence lasts only 7 turns. In each turn, there are X (where X is the number of players) auctions, followed by a couple of actions per player. So during the game each player will win a maximum of 5 auctions and perform exactly 14 actions. And will manage a puzzle-like construction site arranging building and landscape tiles.

The game is very tight, and every auction and every decision matters. It also seems very balanced, with quite a few different strategies available.

I thoroughly enjoyed my first play, and I'd love playing again soon. Too bad I feel it really shines only with 5 players.

RATING: 9
PoF is definitely a favorite of mine, but only if I'm playing with experienced players and if I am using the Princess & Muse expansion.  With new players it is essential to fully explain basic strategies and auction values.  Otherwise the game loses its charm!  My advice for new players of the standard game is to purchase a Profession Card and a Freedom on your first turn.  Some additional advice is to not allow a 1st or 2nd round Jester to go for less than 1,000 Florin.

Although 5 is more optimal than 3 or 4, there are still some balance issues in a 5-player game.  For starters, Player 2 has an advantage in acquiring an extra Profession card.  Another balance issue is that the selection of a 1st Round Profession Card is only 2 cards for Player 5, whereas Player 4 has a selection of 3 cards to choose from and so forth.  However, amongst experienced players, the advantages/disadvantages seem to be minimal for they know how to work the system.         

3 players, 12 plays!
4 players, 10 plays!
5 players, Best, 4 plays!


Princess & Muse = 9 rating
Co-operative Buidling = 8 rating
Standard Princes = 7 rating



[b]Princess & Muse[/b] 
This expansion features an additional auction phase for 6 special characters that takes place before the action phase of each round.  The additional auction provides even more strategies and tension.  I could easily play this game every week.  If you do not own this expansion, a print-and-play version is available on BGG in the files section. 

3 players, 3 plays!
4 players, 10 plays! (w/Co-op Build 1 play!)
5 players, Best, 21 plays! (w/Co-op Build 1 play!)



[b]Co-Operative Building[/b]
In this expansion, players may place a building in such a way that it occupies space on a neighboring player's board, so that both players receive the benefit of having the building.  This is by far the most interactive version of PoF and I highly enjoy it.  Unfortunately there is not yet a print-and-play version available on BGG.  I own the Pro Lodo version, so for me, it's a non-issue.

4 players, 1 play! (w/ P & M 1 play!)
5 players. 2 plays!  (w/ P & M 1 play!)

RATING: 8
Very nice design. The auctions and competition for best work provide opportunities for competitive player interaction, while the action phases allow each player to pursue his own strategy relatively independently (though each player still must consider what his opponents are doing to plan for winning best work, to determine the value of a recruiter, to decide how much money to save for the next auction, etc.). Players must have a long-term strategy but need to be tactically flexible as well -- a great mixture of short and long-term planning (tactics and strategy) missing in many Eurogames, which strongly (or solely) emphasize tactics over strategy. Princes of Florence is fairly well-balanced and avoids significant turn-order and king-making problems (e.g., Puerto Rico). (Although based on some forum threads it appears that starting position (seat) does have a significant impact on final score, based on who plays when in the penultimate and final rounds.)

The major flaw in Princes of Florence is that the substance of the game occurs in the subtleties of the play. It is an (or perhaps [i]the[/i]) archetype of the now quintessential 'multiplayer solitaire' Eurogame, but there [i]is[/i] a highly competitive game hidden in plain view. While newer games like Agricola or 7 Wonders make explicit the concept of denying an action space or denying a key card (while at the same time gaining something very concrete by doing so), Princes of Florence forces players to understand that while it [i]seems[/i] that each player can essentially operate independently, the entire game hinges upon the very few opportunities for competition the game provides (competition for scarce resources [e.g., works, recruiter cards], VP-to-money conversions [i.e., maximizing the return on investment for items won at auction], and best work). If a player only thinks of his own plan and doesn't force the other players to pay the 'right price' for an item, another player could easily run away with the game. Princes of Florence requires all players to possess a high level of familiarity with the game mechanics, understanding of the timing and flow of the game, and ability to correctly value items up for auction (including the implicit benefit of denying the item to an opponent). 

None of these comments inherently indicate a flaw in the game design, but rather are meant to illustrate that, relative to other games, Princes of Florence is more difficult to play competitively, and suffers as a result, degrading from an elegant game of subtle competition into 90-minute multiplayer solitaire. 

[i][Rating based on highly competitive play. For 'average' play, rating -> 7][/i]

[Disclaimer: The above are my thoughts on the game itself, not an implicit (or explicit) statement about my own abilities. I am quite certain I would be utterly trounced by many (if not most) players.] (2012-03; revised 2014-02)

RATING: N/A
BOUGHT USED
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Finally got to play this. Really want to play again. I shoulnd't have been so eager to build the first work, since it put my info out there for someone to steal away with a recruitment card. That taught me the lesson that maybe its best to wait and build works later in the game when they are worth more. I also majorly screwed up my building/park placements. It's important not to do that in this game. I need to play this again.

Played again. Best to get professions of similar buildings. If not possible, then get jesters. Start out with big building. Also good to know all the cards, bonus and prestige. Build work within first two turns. 

Played again. Was able to get 3 jesters and 2 bonus cards. Got insanely outbid for a recruitment card in the last round that would have allowed me to build two works in one turn, a 20 & 23. Values of items were not clearly understood by opponents, hence the lack of competition for the jesters. I still think a lot is determined by what you choose to keep from the initial draw. You need to match factors on the cards. Not just any factors, but the highest like the building type. I was able to win the game handily with just two buildings and a landscape, but all three freedoms and then the jesters.

Save bonus cards for when you really need them and because they will factor more highly when you have the stuff they require. Draw prestige early to give you some direction. Turn order determines who gets to build the work last and thus determine need for competition to gain the greatest work bonus.

If going last in turn 1, then try to build greatest work, otherwise use turn 1 to set up for  turn 2 build, unless you can build two works in turn 3.

Setup
1. draw cards, discard
2. auction
3. action
4. build works

RATING: 6
Princes of Florence is an half-successful attempt at doing an eurogame that’s a bit less «euro» than the usual: it succeeded only partially though, for the system still doesn’t allow any mistake and openly favours the leader. Nonetheless I found the theme satisfyingly developed and the whole game, still too dry for my taste, quite interactive, mainly thanks to a wicked auction mechanics.

What I completely disliked though - and this is also one of the biggest problems I have when approaching any eurogame - is both the need for tracking every player’s points at the end of each turn and the usual cards bonanza allowing to readjust a player’s score and strategies, which means you’re invited to play with your mind rather than your heart: this is stuff for professional optimizers, not for people (even hardcore gamers) who only wish to have fun with a solid game.

RATING: 6
This might have been a great game. it's exciting to look at and learn the rules seeming to combine so many lovely features, including a puzzle like arrange the buindings and stuff in your courtyard bit and a really nice little theme. 

Having said that, the game balance is mundane. You play the game with almost no interaction with other players - mostly solo, except for the auction phase each turn. The puzzle like element is hardly present, in reality and there is shared terrain with other players to make placement of buildings and landscapes interesting - all on your own little solo board. The theme isn't really evoked sduring play, much rather it's a game that encourages endless math tinkering optimising the spending of money - predicted return on invesstment is what it's all about, i avery tight 7 turn game. 

You play only 7 turns, so don't have a lot of things to do really, but a real lot of time to wait on people to make decisions all the same I found. 

I see so many elements of truly excellent game design here, yet so few of them seem to provide me with much of what I'd call fun or entertainment. If only there were one board with limited space which you competed for and more compulsion to build (I saw someone win this with only 1 building until the last turn for example - you can flower and succeed without having to build alas) then tehre might be some conflict and iteraction to make this game for several people. As it stands you could just as well play it alone and enjoy it as much as with 5.

I have to conclude - nice try, but not a favourite by any shot and a slight mystery to me why so many people love it so much. But tthen I said the same about the Settlers of Catan and clearly I'm not a sheep herd Eurogamer it seems. For my part I'll stick to the likes of Adel Verpflichtet, El Grande, Dos Rios, Inkognito, Mississippi Queen, Torres etc. etc. all games which come to mind that I'd rather play than this one ...

RATING: 9
Simply a beautiful game, both in theme and mechanics. There is a little more player interaction than seems apparent, especially in the bidding. There seems to be many ways to approach the path to victory - I have seen the players with the most builders or jesters lose the game because they didn't balance all their acquisitions. Prestige and recruitment cards are very important to acquire, though sometimes ignored in bidding wars for jesters and other items. One key feature is to balance the works reward between points and money. Attention to other elements such as positioning buildings in the pallazo, and obtaining extra recruitment cards are also vital to a winning combination. UPDATE: Was my number 1 ranked game in top ten for five years until bumped down by Merchants and Maurauders.

RATING: 10
A great game. One of my favourites. Well balanced. Elegantly designed. Beautiful pieces. A game that relates to the theme quite nicely.

Best with 5 players. I didn't care for it with 4 players.

I have both the RGG version and the new 2007 expansions (Quined/Ystari version). Brillant expansions by Kramer and Ulrich. The Princess & Muse expansion adds an Amun Re style auction that adds tension and more management. There was a lot more player interaction and depth of play. The other 4 players who played this expansion with me were all experienced PoF players and all agreed that this expansion complemented the base game and greatly enhances it with new texture and nuances. Actually, it finishes this magnum opus, which without it, is incomplete.

Do not neglect the meta game. It can play an important part in one's game.

Rating for base game: 10
Rating with Princess & Muse expansion: 10+

Any way you add it up, The Princes of Florence is a top notch game. Highly Recommended!!!

Update: after many plays with the Princess and the Muse expansion, cannot go back to vanilla PoF, which has become too formulaic. The P&M expansion truly adds multiple paths to victory. With the expansion, this is Kramer's MAGNUM OPUS!!!

RATING: 8
I keep upping my rating of Princes of Florence bit by bit the more I play it.  Princes of Florence is the ultimate game of opportunity costs (moreso than Goa!).  Through the course of the game you get seven auction items and 14 actions and that's it!  At the end of the auction on turn 4 if you don't have your last 8 actions planned out you will probably not win.  Some say the game is dry and "multiplayer solitaire" but there is so much more to it than that.  You hash out a plan and suddenly you have an opportunity in front of you that forces you to rearrange your thinking.  Princes of Florence is a true nailbiter and seems to have near-universal appeal to gamers who have cut their teeth on the likes of Settlers or Carcassonne or Ticket to Ride and are hungry for something meatier.  I now rate this game a solid 9.

RATING: 7.5
I enjoy this game with just two.  We all know that it shines with five, but I'm an Alea addict who can't get enough of their Big Box series.  With a few modifications, it is much more competitive than the official two player variant.  We simply play with 3 each of the landscapes, 4 each of the builders/jesters.  For buildings, use 1 each for the medium and large buildings, with a random extra 3 buildings added for medium, 1 extra for large.  Finally, when drafting cards, only take 3 to pick from when drafting from the Bonus and Prestige decks, rather than the normal 5 (Professions remain unchanged).  The game becomes much more about economization of moves and choices rather than a chaotic free-for-all, but that's fine with me.  This is the game I wanted Leonardo to be!

RATING: 6
UPDATE: A few recent plays have led me to believe that this game is even more overrated than I remember. There really is only about one way to play this game (Jesters/Substitutes), and I don't see anyway in which buying even one architect, never mind two or three, could possibly pay off. Still not bad, once you throw out a couple underpowered bits there is still enough of a game left to be interesting, but in my opinion this is one of the weakest entries in the alea line, weaker even than many of the small-box games. 
---
UPDATE: The one variant in the new ProLudo/QWG edition that isn't available elsewhere, the deal-making version, seems suspect. It so dramatically increases the value of the "substitution" cards (as two neighbors can share a big building and pass the artists back and forth) that it seems to throw that part of the system out of alignment. My judgement is that for English-language users, the new edition is not worth it. Also, playing Princes of Florence again after a long break was less rewarding than I thought. For an alea game (and a Kramer game), the elements seem surprisingly out-of-whack; the architects are too weak, jugglers too strong, and in general there aren't a lot of ways to approach this game and the various different artists do not provide as much game variety as one might hope.
---
UPDATE: Note on the ProLudo/QWG edition: I got this new edition largely for the boards which flip over to reveal one, large principality in which players can negotiate to share the costs of buildings on the borders, therefore adding a much-needed element of player interaction. Hopefully it won't bog things down too much.

One thing which surprised me, they went to some length to internationalize the Work cards, with nice symbols for the landscape, jester, freedom, etc., that give the work points (the freedom is borderline-indistinguishable, but still). So I was surprised that the Prestige and Bonus cards retain their original all-text format when they could have been easily internationalized as well. Players with both editions can easily substitute the cards from the Rio Grande edition, but still.

Anyway, I look forward to the new variants - especially the cooperative-building one - getting this classic game back to the table a bit.
---
This is a good game, I won't deny that, and I do enjoy playing it. But it does nag at me; I dunno, I do think of gaming as a social activity, and in Princes of Florence there just is no sense of actually playing with other people. You just build your little fiefdom, keep your artists happy, and that's about it. There is a very small amount of competition for a couple scarce resources, but very little you can do about it; it's all based on player position (so if I'm player number 1, I can't count on getting two freedoms of my choice, player #3 is in the best position vis-a-vis freedoms, and it's best to be 2nd or 3rd for Personality Cards ...). So while this is a good game and the various optimizations you have to do are at least interesting, amongst the alea games it certainly has nothing on Ra or Taj Majal, where you actually have to interact with, like, people. A lot of people have claimed this is one of the best games going, and I'd just to inject some sanity here by pointing out that for most players, I believe Princes of Florence isn't anywhere near to being in the same league as classics such as Modern Art, El Grande, Settlers, Starfarers, Ra, or Lord of the Rings, simply due to the downtime and analysis paralysis problems. That being said, I know there is a set of fairly hard-core gamers who actually like to try to get the human factor out of their multi-player games. A good acid test is whether you prefer Medici or Modern Art; if you preferred Medici, Princes of Florence will probably be your game. If Modern Art, you'll probably want to look elsewhere.

[Game Surplus $25.50 Jul 2011]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Updated 5/14/11.

I've now played The Princes of Florence twice, roughly a year and a half apart.  Both games were highly enjoyable.  I think that Kramer and Ulrich did a truly fantastic job designing this game.  Everything is tight and very clean.  

One of my primary concerns with auction games (on a general level) is that inexperienced players won't know how to value items, leading to mistakes that can really weaken he whole experience.  The value of nearly any acton in Princes of Florence, however, is very nearly calculable.  New players should theoretically be able to jump right into the fray, assuming that they are competitive enough to work out those rough calculations.

The most significant drawback to the game, in my eyes at least, is the art and production quality.  Everything about the game feels incredibly dated, which makes me less excited to own the game.  Like Puerto Rico, this is a good game that reliably produces entertaining sessions, but that doesn't scream for a place on my shelf.  I know it's probably silly to care so much about components, but that's one of the factors that drives my purchase and play decisions.  I like to fiddle with high-quality bits. 

Although some have complained that the the works in Princes of Florence lack variation, and I had a similar thought at first, I came to recognize that the variation comes in the fact that larger buildings appear on more works. Thus, the larger buildings become more desirable because they can save actions (fewer buildings for more works), but they take up more space and the works associated with them are in greater demand at the table. This gives everyone the same incentives, and puts the emphasis on understanding what your opponents are attempting do. Variations in the value of works themselves would increase the emphasis on figuring out what your own incentives are (akin to deciphering your starting hand in Agricola), and would hinder the ability of your opponents to meaningfully interpret your in-game decisions.

Additionally, both Goa and The Princes of Florence employ the Auction->Actions sequence. Although both have something of a bland, dated feel, I really enjoy the tension that comes from the auctions when the subsequent available action sequence is so obvious and understood. In something like PowerGrid, I never calculate out all of my opponents' possible moves (and their costs) before the auction. In Goa and PoF, I can and do, which makes both games much more interesting to me than they might be oerwise.

Yet, despite these gripes, FvF is a fine game. Though given all the praise, I won't pretend that my expectations weren't disappointed. A 5 in terms of how I enjoyed it, a 6 due to being a good design. Just not what I consider thrilling gameplay.

RATING: 6
PoF is an efficiency game similar to Puerto Rico and belongs to a class of games sometimes referred to as "sandbox" games or "multiplayer solitaire" games. The term comes from the fact that there is little direct player interaction in these games, usually limited to just an auction round. Yet I find I prefer games with more direct player interaction, whether it be conflict in a wargame or more subtle interaction found in negotiation games. Nonetheless, it's a beautiful game and a beautiful, elegant design that's easy to learn and plays quickly.

I should note that my geek buddies rank this game more highly than I do, pointing out its depth and long term strategy. I agree with rorschah however, that what they're really referring to is long term analysis rather than long term strategy. He describes the difference better than I can in his geeklists. I'll also point out that I've only played this with 3 players but it's supposed to be best with 4 or 5.

RATING: 8
Deeply strategic, much like other packing games (for instance, Antiquity) I imagine you will want to have the whole thing planned out before you even start the first round (obviously, in my only play of the game, I wasn't able to do that). As such, I do end up worrying about replayability (for fear that certain options are just plain old stronger than other ones). However, the auctions mean that you still have to be open to adjustment because the plans of others might get in your way.

It is also important to note, that it is fun to make your plans. To try to maximise your fourteen moves. Yes, it's all about efficiency, but I suspect the lack of complexity makes it interesting to me. It's not about efficiency even though the rules get in your way, it's about efficiency even though the other players get in your way.

RATING: 6
Pretty good game where long-term strategy plays a big role compared to tactical moves. The game features different mechanics centered around an auction, but you also need to plan where to put the stuff you win in your small playing area so it has a spatial element too. 

As with most other euro games there are so much you want/need to do but not enough actions! 
The limited amount of rounds and the limited access to a lot of money make it a tight game which is something I like.

As with most auction games, it can be hard to value the different items the first time you play, but I think we managed okay. The jesters are good, but not overpowered as some tend to think. Each item is good in different areas, and if you can pick up a landscape for 200 it might be worth it compared to paying 1000+ for a Jester.

I feel that PoF is a game you can really explore in depth, and there's much more to the game than what first meets the eye. It's a good game, but I do think the game shows sign og age and I have to say that there are many other eurogames, even eurogames with auctions, which I would rather play.

RATING: 6

My criterion for rating games


http://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/147053/item/2300390#item2300390


1) Sense of wonder: Not much variation from game to game, but there is a little bit with the setup of your board and how your opponents will bid for various items.

2) Meaningful/fun decisions. A lot of decisions each turn that are fun and affect your score tremendously especially figuring out how much to pay for items.

3) Player interaction:  A good bit of interaction through auctioning and the different works of art and what order players put them out in.

4) Components/artwork:  Very nice looking components. The style of artwork is unique and neat to look at.

5) Game length/complexity: Good, short game that is not too hard to teach to new players, but still provides a great amount of choices.

RATING: 10
My groups first 'heavy' game, and thus we played this one into the ground. Great game of move management and long term planning; with dedicated play auctions become interesting punch - counter punch affairs with plenty of red herring bids and intrigue afoot.  Though the game can become formulaic with such extreme dedication (never kept track, but we easily played this 50-75 times, if not more, as we often played sessions of 5+ games in a row...) it still holds a fascination and I always want to pull it out when 4 or 5 players are available. The only minor knock on this title is that to really work, like all auction games with these kind of dynamics, it requires a group of semi-equal experience, if the other players aren't aware of what items are worth then auction results can get skewed and things can get a tad out of hand. Easy to solve issue though, just play it more :-)

I might add that the reason this game stands out for me is the fact that it is almost a purely strategic game, as opposed to the more standard tactical fare that one expects from Euro games. While there is a measure of tactical response (i.e.: jacking an auction, discarding one avenue for another when the auction goes awry or the cards come up which force one to) but the game still is largely about properly assessing one's opening hand, and then coming up with a strategy for using that hand as effectively as possible. This really is a top tier game, and is still my favourite pure Euro style game going. A fantastic title that I still want to play every game night.

RATING: 5
There was a time when I loved this game...but after a while, the strategies become repetitive and the game turns out to be little more than an efficiency exercise with the only player interaction being auctions.  I'm really sick of the "5 players but only 4 items" type of interaction that seems to be popular with Euro designers.  It's a shame that the game becomes so dull and offers little actual depth because it's a fun theme and it looks great.  I guess it might be deep and rich to someone who plays Ticket to Ride regularly, but for someone like me that grew up playing Civilization it just isn't.  Not a bad Euro overall- I do like the building of the Villa and some of the decisions regarding which works to go after are compelling, but I'll likely never play it again.

RATING: 9
very enjoyable game and quickly becoming one of my top favourites. This game is all about using actions wisely. You will always want to do more than you are able to. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Die Fürsten von Florenz is one of the few games where I absolutely don't mind the auctioning mechanism. This is probably because the auctions have very strict rules with given starting prices and increments. Also eventually each player will get one (and only one) item from each auction. And since Die Fürsten von Florenz is a game of continuous shortage situations, everything you manage to get your hand on will probably be of value. Usually I also don't like games where you constantly want to do three times as much as you are allowed to. But for some reason this also works very well in Die Fürsten von Florenz. While there are many different options of what to go for, it's usually pretty easy to figure out the pros and cons of each option as well as its ultimate worth in victory points. The game feels very balanced except for the jester tiles which are very powerful. Of course this can easily be compensated by higher auction bids as long as all of the players are aware of the problem. So most of the time there's not only the bad feeling of "I haven't done enough" but also the good feeling of "I probably did the best thing to do with my limited capacities" making Die Fürsten von Florenz one of my all-time favourites. (This is for the alea (2000) edition.)

RATING: 6
I have played about a dozen games to date at all players numbers, but the game simply refuses to ignite the spark of the gun powder keg, and I have no idea why. When I first bought the game, I had to make do with the Alea-edition, which  like every Alea-game is bland and not printed on very 'playable' material. The recent re-release by QWG—for which I provided the Dutch rules translation—makes the material more pleasant to handle, although there are still some minor usability issues. Of course there is more to the game than just artistic design, but the more I play it, the more I am convinced the concept of 'prestige' is something which does not easily translate into an exciting subject. It is quite abstracted and quantified, which is sort-of at odds with the entire meaning of the word 'prestige'. It pushes PoF's underlying mechanisms (auctioning, actions) closer towards the limelight than I would like. 

Not that the game is in any way 'bad'. It is a tight affair, victories can be close, there is some tension in how to play the psychological game of picking an object you don't really want but someone else might removing that player as a competitor for your 'true' auction, and I can recognise that there are multiple strategies you can attempt—as with any good game I'm 'noticing' these on my own. Game-technically, there isn't that much wrong apart from relatively long player downtime in the 5-player version.

But it simply refuses to appeal to me. I prefer the rock'n'roll of Goa, which is just as artificial, and probably weaker design-wise because of the perceived dominant expedition card-strategy (although I have yet to see that happen). My response is always 'meh', even if I win, and even if my winning was a tight long-term affair. And after playing all these games, I still don't know why. As a consequence I'm at a loss how to rate this game. How does one arrive at a number when one is able to clearly recognise the game's good design qualities, but not able to derive enjoyment from playing ...? 

In other words: take the rate with a grain of salt.

RATING: 8
Very cool, clever game design.  Princes of Florence combines auction competition with a tug of war for completing a Palazzo build out and creating works which earn you prestige points.  The player dynamic of using your Florin (money) to buy Jesters, buildings, parks, and other things creates a struggle for getting the most bang for your buck while trying to make your opponents pay too much for the items they're interested in.  Additionally, a neat aspect of the game is how to fit buildings purchased on your Palazo.  Some creativity and spatial accumen is required.  The game flows well and is very solid.  I also like the aspect of the game which incites competition each turn among the players to build the best, most prestigious works.  This is a fun game.  My only negative is that after 20 or more plays, the games originality wears off and play feels like deja vu.  All in all - Princes of Florence is a good fun game worthy of space in one's game collection.

RATING: 9
A perennial favorite in our household, I think it offers a good mix of player interaction and advanced planning.  I would never turn down the chance to play this one.

Players must plan ahead carefully to figure out a combination of purchases and presentations over the course of 7 rounds.  With only a limited number of actions, watching a coveted item sell out or losing a crucial auction can be devastating.

The auction phase of this game isn't as important or intense as in other games (Modern Art, for example).  But this is still a game where you must pay attention to your opponents carefully.  The only negative I have about Princes is the significant downtime between turns; it's not uncommon for the smartphones to come out during slow periods.

RATING: 8

Not many of my group is serious gamers and we all still enjoy this game, which I think says something about how a game can have great strategy and depth, have some learning hurdles, and still be appealing to people who not normally into games like that.  I think the main reason this doesn't get pulled out alot is because of the length of time it can take to play (analysis paralysis can be an issue with this game) and there is a tendency to draw games which play faster, allowing more games in an evening.

RATING: 8
This was one of the first real games I played, and it has a special spot in 8 land as that game forever.  However, this has found new life as... a party game.  Absolutely a 9 as a party game.  Every work that someone plays has to be accompanied with a description as to what they made.  As an evening progresses they start tame (the Mathematician invents geometry so I can have round buildings that will fit on my play mat) go to silly (my Organ maker has a forest and no workshop, so he invents the pan flute) and if you are lucky one entry will go nuts (the Cartographer makes a map to the ancestral home of the Amazons where technology is found for women to overthrow the men of the world) and then every entry after that will be woven in to a grand communal story.  It's a whole nother game.

RATING: 6
Princes of Florence was the other "new to me" game that I played at OGA Con. We got a number of rules wrong and as a result the game essentially eliminated everyone but myself and Eric at one point. They lost the ability to continue to make works, and despite the fact that we let them go back and change a purchase so they could stay in the game, they did not do very well.

The game ended up going over pretty well with my weekend group, and we ended up playing it a handful of times. No one really loved it, however. While I appreciated its design, it did not thrill me, and I came to realize I did not particularly enjoy pure auction games. I like games that have auctions as their focus, but not if the auction is the game. (The one exception of this is Ra, which is an interesting enough game, despite essentially being an extended auction.)

RATING: 8.5
I really enjoy this game on many levels. Theme, artwork, and mechanics are all great. The more games I play by Wolfgang Kramer the more impressed I am.  Definitely my favourite designer. This also seems like a game with enough depth that my skill will have room to grow.  The only downside is that this game is tricky to introduce to new players, for 2 reasons.  First, there is something slightly unintuitive about how works are completed and scored (this certainly makes this game feel a bit different than other Kramer games). Secondly, experienced players have a serious advantage over new players because they will be familiar with the approximate values of the different auction items. To me the ease of introducing a game to new players is an important feature so this accessibility issue is the only thing holding me back from rating this higher.

RATING: 6

The Tetris-like puzzle aspect of the game is a bit odd because it seems separated from what's really going on.  The auction rules are unique, and this is clearly a game from a time when auctions were done in interesting ways.  The game has very low interaction, but it does have long-term planning which some people may enjoy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.25
It's a nifty design that doesn't seem to get much play anymore.  I suspect the old-timers have gotten tired of it, and it's not flashy enough to attract new players.  This isn't a very forgiving game: it's tight and relies on the auction system for balance.  Having a feel for the relative values of things is critical.  There are tense choices as you try and make the most out of every work, fight for most impressive, then have to decide how many of the points you just earned to convert back into cash for future expansion.  If you screw up, you could really shoot yourself in the foot, or give another player a huge advantage.  
The only things I don't really care for are some of the balancing aspects.  It doesn't seem to me that some of the routes are really viable (don't think I've ever seen someone get 3 builders, for ex.  Just not worth it.)  I'm also concerned about how much of the game is determined by the quality of your opening hand, and how many of the requirements dovetail.  Of course, you can always buy the cards that let you re-use other players' luminaries, but not until later in the game, and someone who has everything line up perfectly will still have an advantage.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Very clever game - lots of ways to win. This is a 9 with 5 players - 7 with 3 players. It just plays best with the full allotment of players.

RATING: 9
Princes of Florence is an excellent game. Princes of Florence is the type of game which the more you play, the more you appreciate it...I know I did. :)

The last few times I played Princes of Florence was at Paul's monthly meeting in Laval. I am on a winning streak at the moment.

I haven't played Princes of Florence a whole lot lately, though a couple times more than Puerto Rico.

Something that I enjoy in Princes of Florence more now than I did at the begining is the auctions. What to get in each round and for how much ? Will the other players going to let what you want ? The game has grown on me with more plays.

Some of the items are worth more than others in general. What other people needs can drive the auction high if another person wants the same thing in the same round.

You need to be careful with your money. Sure hate when it happens when you need that 100 or 200 extra to get what you want. It can make a difference if you are tight on money and have no builder.


8 plays since recording games played.

RATING: 8
This game will remind you of a lot of other games, only Princes of Florence probably did it first. Buy stuff in an auction, put it together in interesting ways and score points. The challenge is to have the stuff fit together in multiple interesting ways to be extra efficient.

My one issue with this game is that the endgame falls prey to the Power Card Syndrome, where large amounts of points get handed out to the lucky players that can cash in their bonus points cards, so you could just get a few points or a lot, depending on which cards you picked.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
[Auction/Action, Planning, Art/Renaissance] {3P:9, 4P:10, 5P:10} This is my favorite German game. The Princes of Florence is a beautiful and elegant Kramer/Ulrich masterpiece. Its design incorporates strategy, multiple roads to victory and a fun and immersive theme. The game itself is a wonderful sight to behold, done in the style of Leonardo da Vinci's notebooks and drawings. Outside of its outstanding auction, interaction between players is subtle but as effective as direct conflict. The game works well for all three player configurations. The five-player game is an exercise in dealing with great demand for scarce resources; it is tight and exciting. The four player game is an exercise in plan execution and adjustments; it is fast, fun and addictive. The three-player configuration is an exercise in economy and perfection; it is relaxing and enjoyable. Alas, The Princes of Florence cannot accomodate just two players, but that would be asking for too much. This game cannot be faulted; it is an amazing and exemplary design. ***UPDATE: Reaffirming my 10 rating, and deep appreciation of PoF. It's still the most elegant game I've laid hands, eyes and mind on. While I enjoy playing it online, the experience is diminished. Will suggest this game anytime, anywhere. *** UPDATE: I've written a strategy article. I've played close to a hundred games online. I've played a couple dozen games F2F. I've played three games in a row both F2F AND online. And I still want to play this game more. It's the first game I look for on BSW. It's the first game I suggest on game night, unless there's a new game begging to be tried. If there's a better Eurogame than The Princes of Florence, it hasn't been made yet. Exquisite. *** UPDATE: Will acquire new Quined multi-language edition as backup copy. My alea edition is pretty banged up after so many plays, with many more to come. Was going to get another alea, so might as well get a different edition of my only 10-rated Eurogame.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
The more I play, the more I love this game! I just can't play this enough. The flow of this game is just beautiful and it scratches all the right Euro itches in such a short time. Too short.  

I don't think of this game as multi-player solitaire at all. The auction is crucial to victory, and you're always having to watch the work that other people are completing. There is a lot of tension and interaction. 

Tried it with a 2-player variant. The auction is certainly broken without more. I didn't mind it - (I'll play it solo if I could), but we'll probably never play it with just 2 again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game does not have a significant learning curve (especially when compared to something like Tigris and Euphrates). People new to the game can fairly quickly grasp the concepts and understand why certain actions exist, so strategies start to develop early. Even though it takes a solid 20 minutes to explain the game, everyone can jump right in, and by the second game there will be no problems.

Even with only 14 actions and 7 auctions, this game offers tons of interesting decisions, especially in a 5-player game. The seamless blend of intense auctions, customizable individual player-boards, intuitive point conversions, and interesting player interactions/competitions combine to make a flat-out awesome game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.6
Megpróbálom tárgyilagosan értékelni a játékot és nem figyelembe venni az előkelő helyezését. Sajnos nekem nem igazán jött be, és nem teljesen értem ezt a jó pontszámot, de nem szeretném emiatt jobban lehúzni, mint amit érdemel. :)
Szóval ez valahogy egy tipikus "régi" gémer játék. Már megfigyeltem, hogy ezekkel gyakran nem vagyok kibékülve. A régebbi játékok sajátja szokott lenni valami elegáns, egyszerű központi mechanizmus. Na az itt nincs. Licitálás, akciózás, műkártyák. Ráadásul a pont/pénz osztás valami teljesen értelmetlenül van mindenhol leírva. Az egész egy zagyva saláta. Erre jön még rá, hogy ami a játék végi pontkülönbségeket hozza, azok pont olyan dolgok, amikre tökre nem vagy hatással. Képtelenség 5 főnél kitalálni, hogy éppen ki mire akar majd licitálni. Pedig a lényeg az lehetne, hogy úgy lavírozz, hogy neked mindig olyan dolog kelljen, ami a többieknek nem annyira. Mert vagyonokat fizetni szinte semmiért sincs értelme. 
Még tovább rontja a helyzetet a kártyahúzás randomja. Ha valaki az elején kap három egybevágó műkártyát, akkor elég nehéz lesz annak a dolga ellene, akinek három diszjunkt jutott. Erre jönnek még a presztízs kártyák, amiknek a céljai, pontozása valami teljesen értelmetlen. Az egyik 5 pontot ad, ha neked van a legtöbb parkot, a másik 8-at, ha mindenféle landscape-ed van. Node a többségre 5 főnél képtelenség számolni. Bármikor, bárki unalomból, csak a 3 pontért vesz egy akár második parkot is. A bónuszkártyák közül is van olyan, ami csupa egyforma dologra ad, ami nyilván nem éri meg amúgy más miatt, míg van olyan, ami csupa különbözőre, ami eleve adott általában. 

És mindezek tetejébe jön nehezen tolerálható holtidő (legalábbis 5 főnél). Az akciós köröket végigülni úgy, hogy semennyire se érdekel, hogy mit csinál a másik elég szenvedős. Pláne, mikor 5 kártyákból kell válogatni, meg 5 perceket gondolkodni a pont/pénz elosztáson.

Összességében nagyon aránytalannak érzem a játékot. Bizonyos elemei erősen gémer irányba tolnák, de mások meg teljesen légbőlkapott értelmetlen randomot visznek bele. És ez az, ami valahogy a a fent említett "régi gémer" játékokra jellemző. Végül az egészet nyakon öntjük egy nagy adag holtidővel és kész is a Princes of Florence.

RATING: 6
PoF is a somewhat different auction game. The auctions are crucial and really the central part of the game, but they are very much a means to an end here. Somebody will always get something for cheap, and the remaining player actions can make quite a big splash, too.

Underneath it all, the game is very much strategic. While you can certainly play from turn to turn the need to create successful works (which is the main source of money and points) requires an efficiency that is possible only with long-term planning. By turn 2 or 3 (of 7) a game plan has formed, and from then on you'll try to make it reality.

RATING: 8
Even though this game is over 15 years old it still feels fresh for a Euro. Standard auction mechanic, but the options of what you can buy and working to interconnect all of the pieces is a good deal of puzzle-ly fun.  The best part is the "points or money" mechanic, which works to keep the players in a very tight race.  The pace is a little choppy as players read over the cards they have the ability to take but the game plays out in the right amount of time.  Also, the additional spatial element of placing buildings is icing on the auction / points / income generating system.  All in all this is a classic game and deserving of its continued relevance in the BGG Top 100.

RATING: 2
Very poorly designed game that relies on an auction system to balance costs.  Game doesn't scale well to multiple "thinking" players as you can easily perform exhaustive computations with what information is available on the table.  Being *allowed* to hide money is also a poor choice, as it is thick and not well-suited for hiding.

In the short-term, the game is difficult to understand and unintuitive.  Once this is overcome, the game is still annoying due to the laziness of execution in game design.

Highly not recommended due to arbitrary elements, such as not gaining bonuses for being a jack of all trades, and the pointless "minimum work-value" per round, which is almost never missed.  I had a good laugh trying to imagine a prince giving his patronage as much frustrating thought as a player does in this game.  Terrible.

RATING: 2
Please try this before you buy it.

I honestly don't see why people like this game so much.

1) It has absolutely no theme. In Puerto Rico, or Goa, or many other popular Euro games, I actually feel like I'm doing something related to the game theme.  This one in no way shape or form makes you feel like you are in medieval Florence.

2) The game play is frankly boring.  People take forever to squint at the details on everyone else's work cards to see if they want to steal them.  (Which doesn't really hurt the player being stolen from.) People take forever to place their buildings and such in their grids.  The auction phase is completely unexciting. Producing a work brings little satisfaction.

3) It has almost zero player interaction.  Some say "the interaction is subtle."  The auction and "competition" in this game is the least competitive I've ever seen in a highly rated game.  It is frankly a quiet, non-social game.

I'm sorry, but I just don't get it.  If you've never played this game, please try before you buy.

RATING: 7
RECEIVED: 2016

First-play thoughts after a 3-player game:

PROS
1. I like the balance of player interaction with the auction phase and the personal development of the city-building/purchasing/scoring in the action phase. 
2. There seem to be interesting choices and variable paths to victory. 

CONS 
1. The game has an obnoxious number of parts. This makes set up a chore and players unfamiliar with the game will likely feel confused about what pieces or cards can be bid on in the auction phase and what pieces or cards can be bought in the action phase. It'd be nice to have the board extend to either side of the fame track with spots for these components to make it clearer. 
2. The art is a bit ugly and the font is annoying. The whole game could use a fresh coat of paint. 
3. The auction feels weak because the initial bet is set at 200 and subsequent bets can only be upped by 100. Maybe I'd change my opinion in a 5-player game.

OVERALL
I enjoyed my initial play of PoF but, in the future, I want to play it at 4 or 5 only.

RATING: 7.5

RATING: 7
My view of The Princes of Florence is that it's not quite as good as its reputation. While I admire the game's design, it does have a distinct "solitaire" feel that detracts from my overall enjoyment.  There's also an almost complete lack of tactics (as opposed to strategy), making the game less interesting and less surprising than I would like.

The game's decision points can be broken down into four areas: (1) the auction, which you could almost say "is" the game - certainly it's where the game is won or lost; (2) deciding how many florins to take late in the game; (3) plotting your future actions to ensure that you can accomplish your goals in time and to estimate how many discretionary actions you can spend; and (4) playing Tetris.

#1 and #2 are good stuff, but #3 makes me feel like I'm submitting a business plan or something, since it's a solitaire puzzle which my opponents cannot influence except to make me pay more for auctioned items.  I realize that this exacting planning is what many players love about the game.  I would enjoy The Princes of Florence more, though, if there were more wiggle room allowed in the planning.  I want to be able to adjust to my opponents or stay a bit flexible or fine tune or just have room for some tactical maneuvering.  The best low-luck, strategic multiplayer games allow this (Goa, Age of Steam, etc.) but The Princes of Florence does not.  #4, the Tetris minigame, annoys the hell out of me.  It's critically important to get right, but it seems alien to the rest of the game, like a tumor.  The importance of fitting everything into your palazzo exacerbates the rigidity of the game - the need to plan your whole course of action from the very first turns.  It introduces a huge penalty for players who want to adjust or fine-tune their plans mid-game.  Unless their plans involve building very few buildings, purchase of an inefficient second builder will probably be the penalty for changing your plan or for not having the foresight to have everything mapped out to a "T" from the start.  And I think that sucks.  The upshot of #3 and #4 is that I spend large chunks of the game feeling like an automaton executing my program.

Another unfortunate quality of the game is that it only works well with equally skilled players.  One clueless player will throw the whole thing out of whack.  (People lodge this complaint against Puerto Rico, and they're right, but I think it applies much more severely to The Princes of Florence.)  Finally, unless you have just the right group of people, the game has too much downtime for my taste.

So it probably sounds like I hate the game, but you can see from my rating that I do not.  The auctions (and planning for auctions) are good enough to override many of my other gripes, making The Princes of Florence a respectable "once or twice a year at most" game for me.  My rating is for the five-player game, which is the only configuration that I think is worth playing.  The market for the auctioned items is too loose with fewer players, as is the easy availability of professionals.

RATING: 8
Yera

Eurogame ambientado en la florencia del renacimiento. Somos principes en nuestro palacio y queremos que nuestro palacio sea el mas mejor del vecindario, asi que para ello requerimos a los mejores profesionales (pintores, musicos, etc) los cuales a su vez requieren la construccion de determinados edificios. 

En la practica esto se traduce en basicamente dos fases: una de subasta en la que tratamos de obtener determinados elementos, intentando obtener los que nosotros necesitamos o impedir que los rivales obtengan los propios, y otra de compra en la que adquirimos edificios, todo ello a su vez lo situaremos en nuestro tablero siguiendo determinadas reglas. Con los elementos obtenidos en ambas fases, podremos completar (o no) las cartas de profesionales que tenemos en mano, y en base a ello sumar puntos y obtener dinero para las proximas subastas y compras. 

Todo ello muy bien entrelazado. Y el tema, aunque pegado, casa bien con el desarrollo. 

Es un buen eurogame. Su mayor defecto es que no es visualmente notable (aunque cumple bien), y que requiere 5 jugadores para la mejor experiencia de juego (aunque es jugable para 3+)

RATING: 6
Great game, but almost no player interaction at all.  Other than the auction phase, which is pretty basic, you are just playing your own game and planning what you will do.   The lack of interaction is what really hurts this one.  Plus, If one person gets a lucky draw on profession cards, you may as well stop playing.  The bonus cards, builder, and prestige cards help to keep everyone in the game as even the folks who don't get exactly what they want have plenty of good options.  Money is pretty tight and overspending can really kill you as the money/VPs come from one source.  As a novice player, the jester strategy seemed overly strong, especially if you fish for the jester bonus cards.  I was initially going to rate this game higher, but the more I think about it, the more I think that it is just another solitaire / group circle jerk.  It is really good for what it is, but not good enough to be worth a higher rating.  Other than the auction phase its nothing more than a static optimization exercise, and between skilled players the entire game will be decided by card draws and how you do in the auctions, which is mostly luck since you can't evaluate how important certian things are for other players without seeing their hand.  The downtime also sucks.  Its a game where you find yourself constantly asking if it is your turn yet.  Fun to play once in a while, but not something I would play multiple times in a row.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
This is a bidding game, and suffers from two problems because of that:

1) It bogs down in calculations, both when you bid (because you need to calculate how much you need to spend in the next phase) and when you make works and choose how much money to take (because you need to calculate how much you need for bidding and spending next turn).

2) Inexperienced players who bid for things which won't help them can screw things up for other players who actually have a good strategy and know what they're doing.

But overall, this game can be fun, and is somewhat popular and understandable for newbies.  There is definitely a lot of strategy required to win the game, because you have to choose a path to minimize your resource conflicts with the other players.  The fact that there are multiple ways to win is a big plus.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[Initial Impression: 8] 
I played this again recently after a long break, and was reminded how good it really is.  Some complain about low player interaction, but I think the auction phase of the game belies this experience.  The auctions are crucial in allowing you to complete works, and to play well you must bid against opponents even for items that you don't necessarily want - but an opponent, knowing this, might let you have that not-so-useful item, thus denying you what you really need. The mid-game to end-game can be extremely tense, as completed works score more and more points as the game goes on, and you need money less and less, so laggards can quickly catch up.  I love how you constantly have to trade off money for points, both of which you desperately need, and a lot of the fun comes from trying to work within the system to optimize your own scoring and money management - there's that reckoning at the end of the game where, regardless of who won, you realize that you frittered away precious points for money that you didn't need.  And you really feel disappointed in your planning if you are forced to trade back points for more money.  However, there's also a lot of second-guessing other players, trying to bid up the items they really want and to time playing certain works so that they don't have a good chance to recruit them. Much of the strategy comes in looking for those items that others are undervaluing, while trying not to undervalue items yourself.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Princes of Florence is a classic Euro from Kramer & Ulrich which challenges players with a number of different activities, including auctions, limited actions, limited money, and space fitting, as well as card collection.

There are many paths to victory (points) but not all are equal. Creating works offers the greatest options: money or points. But building in your acreage gives points, and helps raise the minimum value for Works. Jesters also raise the min value for works, as do having several Professions (benefactor-sponsored artists and scientists).

But the designers cleverly created a scale to mimic an educated public which demands greater works as the game progresses, so that players have to improve their estates, grant new freedoms to the people, entertain them with Jesters, or seek other ways to improve the works to higher quality.

This costs money- and great works generate money, but they are also the single biggest source of PP, or victory points. Players must choose between refilling coffers and PP each time a work is produced. Choose too little money, and you will not win the auctions to improve your estate, choose too much and you will fall behind in the struggle to be the greatest dynasty of Florence.

When estates are improved, there are several rules which limit how buildings and landscapes are placed, leading to planning and space-filling strategy, and there is also the opportunity to purchase Prestige cards which grant extra PP at game end for fulfilling particular sets of conditions.

All of this adds up to a game which is in the Top 100, and previously Top 10- it's a classic game, which offers a lot to do in only 7 quick rounds.

RATING: 9
This is a beautifully designed game, one which I can see myself playing and trying to master for a long time. There are several great mechanics -- bidding over useful items, trying to place buildings in a Tetris-like fashion, and choosing which 14 actions to take over the course of the game.

Very simple to learn, but difficult to master. This is a very well crafted game. It probably won't be long before I call it a 10.
