RATING: 10
This is a near-perfect information game. It can be a brain-burner trying to optimize one's moves. And because of that, weaker players making sub-optimal moves can give the advantage to more experienced players. If players are equally matched, this plays almost like chess, where one mistake will cost a player the game. Initial rating '8'.

Update (06/09/09): 7 bidding turns and 14 actions. That's it. Try to squeeze out as many prestige points out of those 7 turns, while preventing your opponents from doing the same. Fascinating.

Update (09/22/09): Why I like this over chess is beyond me, given that they are both near-perfect/perfect information games, brain-burners, and punishing play. I suppose it is because of the variety of options, and deeply immersive play. Chess, in comparison, is a pure, classic abstract. I've played my share of chess in high school, but had moved on.

Update (01/02/11): Disturbing that one player has won issuing seven (7) works! Which means the others (including myself) let him get away with one too many profession cards, and allowing him to get two recruiting cards too cheaply. In this manner, several weak (and new) players could throw the game, similar to the right-weak-player-enabler in Puerto Rico.

Update (09/11/11): This has the table a lot more lately, and with good reason--it is a brilliant, "classic" Euro. While some gamers are occupied by the fad of the new, there are others that are going back and rediscovering what makes German games great. Thank goodness there was a reprint. Upping to a '9'.

Update (12/30/11) 5 plays: The player from 01/11 issued eight (8) works for a crushing win! With two weak players that didn't take advantage of buying professions early or bidding on recruiting cards allowed this player to run away with the game. Another example of weak player binding, similar to Puerto Rico, but it seems not dependent on turn order, but is dependent on number of weak players.

Update (01/02/12) 7 plays: Beat the player from 01/11 at his own game, issuing seven (7) works for 60 pts. Although he only issued five (5) works, combined with best-work bonuses, competitive bidding (making me pay dearly for recruiting and jokers), and one or two prestige cards, he was only 6 pts behind me! So, it is possible to do well with fewer works, but those works had better pay off big.

Update (03/09/12) 9 plays: This game feels and plays differently with 3 players versus 5. There's less checks-and-balances during bidding, and less competition for the profession cards (you could get too many and not be able to play them all, so don't get greedy). However, this does mean that one of the three freedoms may disappear within the first turn! This also means that the valuations for the jesters, builders, and recruiting cards are probably worth less than in a 5-player game, as it is very likely you'll get them cheaply, eventually. While I usually don't like efficiency games, I enjoy this one a lot, especially when approaching it from the 7x auctions, 14x actions perspective. It's holding at a '9'.

Update (10/20/12) 10 plays: Played with "The Muse and the Princess" expansion that came with the reprint, with 5-players. We all enjoyed the expansion tremendously, as it takes a game that we all felt was rather "solved" and scripted, and fixes the issues and opens it up completely in unimaginable ways. The landscapes and prestige cards were largely discounted relative to the power of the jesters and recruiting cards, but the expansion makes builders, landscapes, prestige and bonus cards more competitive. There is a second special auction after usual auction for objects, which involves recruiting special characters, each with special abilities:
1) The Banker gives 600+ florin and allows conversion of florin into PP at the end of the game.
2) The Cardinal gives three actions instead of the usual two.
3) The Merchant allows a player to buy an object after the auction for free, or advances PP depending on player ranking.
4) The Muse gives additional WV to [i]each[/i] work, equal to the current round number.
5) The Princess produces WV depending only on the number of landscapes (+3WV each), builders, buildings, and profession cards (+2WV each).
6) The Professor allows a player to acquire a prestige or bonus card for free.

Players bid on these characters by assigning bids on the spaces on the cards, ranging from 300 to 1500 florin, with the Banker being the exception whose bid spaces range from 0 to 500 florin. The bid mechanism is similar to Amun-Re, where if you are outbid, you are kicked off the character card, but you can rebid on the same character or switch to another. If the bid comes around to a player, and no one has outbid, that player can immediately get the character. This is similar to the cascading auction of 18XX.

Having these characters/abilities significantly changes the tried-and-true (and arguably boring) strategies, and breathes fresh air into the game. The game is a bit longer because of the additional auction rounds, but no one noticed the time going by, because it is now even more absorbing!

The relative value of each character changes throughout the 7 rounds, and also emphasizes having more cash on hand, especially for the last round. Normally, players are concerned about converting all their cash into PP, making sure to not have any cash left at the end of the game. However, now there is incentive to hoard cash for the final character auction, where a player can get locked out of a valuable character with a maximum, preemptive bid of 1500 florin!

The Banker now is a source of cash, while the trading PP for florin mechanism is removed. However, having him at the end of the game allows him to convert all of his florin into PP! For example, 1800 florin could become 9 PP!

The Cardinal gives an extra valuable action, meaning that players have more flexibility and time in setting up their infrastructures (in terms of getting bonus cards and buildings) to make very high WV work plays during the later rounds.

The Merchant adds a twist into the next object auction round, as the player could get the object for free. Thus, usually expensive objects, such as jokers, recruitment cards, and builders become somewhat discounted. If a player wins the Merchant with a 300 florin bid, he can then inflate the bidding for a joker next round, thereby making other players weary of overbidding.

The value of the Muse increases steadily throughout the game, adding 7WV to each work produced during the last round! Thus, bidding for this character is aggressive, meaning players will hoard at least 1500 florin to compete for this card.

The Princess incentivizes players to get more landscapes, so that a player could produce a work without using a valuable profession card. Buildings, builders, and profession cards help too, but bonus cards and jokers cannot be used. With the right initial plays, she can crank out a 7WV "work" during the first round without having to expend a valuable profession. Instead, you would have more time to set that profession up.

The Professor makes going after prestige cards worth it, since now you have the opportunity to get several prestige cards early in the game, and then set up your infrastructure to profit from it. In my first game, I quietly acquired an unheard of six cards, which became approximately 30 PP!

Playing PoF with this expansion is highly recommended. With it, this easily becomes a '10'. This is one of the best expansions for any game I have ever experienced. It makes a great game, even better, without significantly altering the play or adding additional rules complexity.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
One of the best games from the Alea line. That said, I'm not a great fan of auctions being used as a leveller between game choices of widely differing value. They only work when everyone at the table is aware of the value of a commodity, and when that's true the winning bid will fall within quite a small range. The player actions represent more of a puzzle, but there are plenty of paths to victory so you're rarely out of contention. It really needs all 5 players for the auction to work, but then you're struggling to get Profession Cards so I'm conflicted as to the optimum number of players. This is unquestionably a very clever game, but it's almost entirely abstract and mostly an optimisation puzzle with minimal interaction. I prefer the stronger theme to mechanism link of games like Puerto Rico, but you can't say this isn't worth playing.

RATING: 8
One of the first German games I played, The first game pulled out at NBG at St Andrews. I loved it and we all played it again the second week. I have introduced several people to German games by playing this timeless beauty. You can plan your tactics but if you get out foxed at the auction everything will be undone. Auction games are tough with my wife as she will not let go (most of the time) I always end up over paying for everything and sometimes get stuff I dont want when she hops out.

Not much interaction in this game, I dont like playing it often or with people who play it often, I dont like knowing or planning what I will do for the whole of the game right from the start. I prefer games that have a greater element of strategy reasponce. If I play this game rarely I find I like it - more often and I KNOW how to get my best score and that just isnt fun for me.

RATING: 4
Princes of Florence is your typical in-the-vein-of-Puerto-Rico style Euro with mechanics that must be studied and calculated instead of gingerly skipped around in.  It's extremely tough on newbies since some of the actions and auction items are worth far more than meets the eye on the first outing.  I generally don't like dry and uninteractive games like this where you can be totally hosed out of foward progress by an unforseen barrage of unanticipated variables.  Of course, I also didn't really get the whole point of the player boards for whatever they added exactly, but that's just me.  I was told I would hate it, so I can't say it failed my already lowered expectations...it just didn't dazzle them all that much.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The Princes of Florence has successfullly combined the theme and the game mechanics. Players take the role as a director to lead prestigious family in Florence at the age of the Renaissance. Gamers cultivate men of talent in various area, such as art, music, technics, and academic etc. Game participants cannot take their eyes off from the everchanging game circumstances. Not only offering various tactical options, but also the game provides dynamical challenges. Mainly based on strategy game mechanics, The Princes of Florence also includes auction and bidding system. The game has well balanced system both strategic and luck factors. This game is the best choice to fully enjoy Wolfgang Kramer and Richard Ulrich's masterpiece.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Ideal Players: 3-4

Description: Build buildings, landscape your grounds, and provide freedoms to provide the best environment for your artists and scholars. 

Pro: Wide variety of actions provide interesting choices. Bidding and limited buildings/freedoms force player interaction. Prestige cards provide different goals for victory. Well-balanced game with very little downtime.

Con: Relatively dry theme. Limited player interaction.

RATING: 9
With a very attractive theme and nice components this would be a good game to introduce to non-gamers, if it were not so involved. Few decisions are easy as there are numerous play options and every aspect of the game is interlinked. This means that you really have to plan carefully in order to maximise your scoring. However, interaction between the players is limited to the auction phase and to the competition to create the work with the highest value. After 10+ plays I've finally increased my rating to a 9. Princes of Florence starts to show its subtlety and balance when all players know the game well and can therefore execute strategies and refine their tactical play, while taking into account what the other players are doing.

RATING: 7.5
Just great game - many decisions, several paths to choose from, a lot of optimization.  It's demanding and complex (though not in terms of rules).

Visibly better for 5 players -> more interaction, more tension, more fun...

EDIT: I'm beginning to worry that with 3 players only 1 viable strategy exists...  Another reason to play the game with 5.

Expansions (I own them as a part of the Polish edition):
- 'Princess & Muse' - adds more complexity and tough choices, but what's more important - ensures true replayability and prevents schematic, the only correct strategies.  I think it's a MUST for experienced players.
- 'Cooperative building' - cannot say anything as I haven't played it yet, but it's not so promising on paper

EDIT2: After 10+ plays (some with expansion) I can say I've seen enough.  The replayability becomes an issue.  I appreciate the design, I can occasionaly play it, but I don't have to own the game.

Sold.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Highly strategic with multiple paths to victory and very little luck, I love the fact that when you pick a card you pick up 5 cards and choose the one you want minimizing the luck. 

Similiar feel to GOA where you are auctioning for things to build your own board and everyone is fighting for the same things. One of the best auction and building games I've played and one that requires you to have a game long plan and adjust along the way, similiar to Taj in that way, another outstanding Euro.

The solitaire complaint here has a little validity as something like Leonardo has shown however the auctions are highly interactive/competitive between gamers and you need to keep your eye on the other players throughout. Fun in a competitive way and certainly a top-notch Gamer's game 

[u]UPDATE:[/u] 9 => 9.5 (probably a 10, more games to confirm) Well, well, it was bound to happen. I mean it had a 9 rating for me all along as I immediately recognized the brilliance but POF was actually a slow burner for me as my desire to play has really increased the more I play it. I have seen multiple ways win this game and it's fun trying each one as you make a plan and 'hope' that the auctioning will go your way so you don't have to deviate too much or over spend to keep on track. This is really the challenge of the game as it pits you against your opponents directly in the auction and it is 'that challenge' that brings me back for more. 

Another beautifully crafted Euro that demands game-long planning and always plays tense and close between good gamers 

[u]UPDATE:[/u] Finally rose to a 10 (My 3rd), the game starts becoming FUN actually in repeat playings with the same players. You get to know their 'tricks' and their limits and it is damn fun trying to push them over them or just plain try to get them off their game. The game itself is very unforgiving and beautifully balanced at the same time, the unforgiving part keeps you on the edge of your seat while trying to stay on course and still pick things up at the prices you want, TOUGH in the right crowd. I have to agree with John(verandi)on two good points here, the game can feel formulaic which is kinda good and maybe a little bad and that winning definitely "often comes down to the little things". Definitely a 5 player game, 4 is good but no where near as 5. On another note there are some very funny moments and comments made in the game when something goes way too cheap or you end up paying 800 for a bluddy park! 

KRAMER'S MASTER "WORK" & GERMAN GAMING PERFECTION 


[b]Princess and Muse expansion:[/b]

While POF signifies gaming perfection for gamers, and it is pretty hard to top perfection...this is a truly an excellent exp!  Princes is a 10 w/o the expansion however this not only gives the game TONS more replay value but also adds more to the already multiple paths, LOVE it with the only downside of it also adding a little length (maybe 20 to 25 minutes in a 5 player game).  

The new things to bid on in the second auction are really interesting and can help you where and when you really need it. The different abilities can influence how you shape your gameplan and really add to the things to think about here. While you could say the game is 'not as tight' as these do allow some flexibility, I have played several times and the endgame scores have been surprisingly pretty close just as in the regular game. 

There is a lot of leap frogging in this new Amun-re style auction where you bid on one item and can be outbid only here, you can rebid on the same item which is where it differs from the Amun-Re one.  One little negative is that not all items are good in all rounds but that makes those rounds really hard to get something so even more competitive where sometimes you just have to 'bid it up' and then possibly get stuck bowing out with nothing.  Awesome! 

[u]UPDATE:[/u] Well the expansion really makes the game an 11 and would never play without it. After many plays though there is one "small" issue, could be group dependant not sure. You play 2.5 hours and in the very last auction, you have to "hope" to get what you need and not get screwed out of it by chance. Granted if you are watching everyone you can usually anticipate what they are going to do when experienced players but not all the time. So do you have to have a plan "B"? Yeah you can and I guess it mostly works. My thought is to just be able to buy what you want in the last auction only to reduce the chance effect. Just a thought I am toying with since I dont like surprise endings to long multiplayer strategy games. 

[u]Tidbit[/u] Like the blind auction variant for turn order, losers get the money back, bid for each seat. This means not getting the extra work in the 5 player game for "free".

Initial Rating: 7.0 (August 2008)

I was expecting so much from this famous game, that I can’t help to be a little disappointed it wasn’t fun enough for me.  I have not played it with 5, so there is still hope for the game (although I’m wary of the downtime). Right now I can say that the game is easy to teach, it has some interesting decisions in it, but it has some (unwanted) luck involved in the game with the professions and the prestige cards.

It is a good game, but I was expecting a GREAT game. The theme is very boring for me.

For now, I’m willing to play this again if a friend asks for it, but I don’t plan to pursue further plays, I just sold my copy of the game. If I want to play an Eurogame, I would play Agricola or may other game over this one.

RATING: 7
This is a tough one for me to rate. While I do appreciate the game as an excellent design and I would never turn down a game, there are a couple of elements that keep it from being one of my favorites. I just feel the game is too short and too much driven by my starting hand of professions. Most of my 7 auctions and 14 actions in the game are driven by what is on those first 3 cards. Certainly there is still room for some clever play, mostly in deciding what to collect early in the game versus late, but I still feel too anchored at times by the objects listed on those initial cards. 

Also, even though money is a large part of this game, the values, except for the auction, are all set, so rather than making evaluation decisions, you are mostly deciding how to divide income and points. While I appreciate this mechanic, it doesn't capture my imagination like really trying to set a value on something. Value is translated in this game into spacial terms (ie. a large building is more expensive because of the space it takes, not what it costs, but larger buildings also support more professions which increases their value - depending on your professions). To me, the professions in hand often take the work out of really having to value something. If its listed in your hand, its valuable to you, and in most cases you don't need to decide HOW valuable, because the price is set. Simple as that.

RATING: 7
El juego consta de dos fases, una de subasta y otra de acciones. En la primera los jugadores pelean por 7 tipos de elementos y en la segunda aplican 2 de las 5 acciones disponibles. Los objetos conseguidos en la fase de subasta y algunos de los conseguidos en la fase de acción, pueden ser usados en esta última fase.

Además de estas dos fases, puedes añadir otra fase más ente ellas. En esta fase los jugadores pujan por personajes, cada uno con un poder diferente.

Hay otra opción extra, los jugadores pueden colaborar compartiendo edificios en sus tableros.

Es un buen juego,aunque creo que como mejor funciona es a 5, ya que con menos gente siempre hay objetos disponibles.
[color=green]
LAST PLAY: 2010
[/color][color=blue]
The game has two phases, an auction phase and an action phase. In the first one, players fight for the 7 different kind of elements and in the second one, they play 2 of the 5 available actions. In the second phase you can play some of the objects you got in the auction and in the action phase.

Besides these two phases, you can add another phase between them. In this phase players bid for characters, each one with different powers.

There is another extra option, players can cooperate sharing buildings in their boards.

It's a good game, but I think it does its best with 5 players, with less people there are always availabe objects.[/color]

RATING: 8
[b]First Impression[/b] (3 plays, 9 rating): Wow, this one made a big splash. Some of the highest production values I've seen in any game. HUGE potential for creativity with emergent narratives. Stiff competition for resources. Lots of options. Even a small spatial element (a bit of tetris on your own board).  

[b]Revised comment[/b]: the imaginative episodes that come out of this game are, surprisingly, one of the strongest things about it, but the more you play it, the less you notice (because it's incidental narrative, not decision-driven narrative). Players will arrive at a point where it becomes a pretty blunt exercise: 7 auctions, 14 actions. Yes, it's all very slick and well put-together. Long-term strategy is paramount. Auctions are tough. The game just doesn't have a whole lot of [i]range[/i]. 

So: Precise, Challenging, Positional, Long-term, and ultimately Narrow, with a nifty representation of patronage that recedes into the background, since who-likes-what is so straightforward and who-works-alongside-who doesn't have any gameplay implications.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
You are trying to fulfill one to three requirements of building, landscape, and freedom on profession cards to score points. Fulfilling more of the requirements gives a better score. Each round has an auction when you can buy things to help scoring, some examples being parks, lakes, and jesters. Then on your turn you can do various actions such as buy certain buildings, buy freedoms, or take new profession cards, or score the professions. As long as you meet the minimum score for the round, you collect cash when you score a profession or can take victory points instead. The minimum scores keep getting higher each round.

RATING: 8

21/4/08 Just finished 4 straight games of Princes.  My evaluation has completely changed; I now rate this a 9 (it may almost be a 10 but will have to see how it holds up under repeated play).  The only drawback is that you are engaged in your own little world and there is very little interaction with the other players.  Other than that the mechanics are very very smooth.  The decisions are so varied; "oh, do I bid up the Jester to force the other player to take it for a high price, take my cheep forest/park/lake, or recruitment card so I can steel a work, go for prestige for some important bonus points, or take the builder for a monetary gain later in the game?  AND, if I don't take one of those, how will it affect me later?  It's brilliant!!  Over four consecutive games, the biggest point spread at the end was maybe 8 points from first to third place.  
   Things there is a real value in producing the highest WV work each turn for the oh so important 3 PP bonus.  Usually the points differences were excatly equal to the number of bonus points each player got.  As the game progressed the number of Jesters each player has equalizes so the only way to get the bonus 3PP is by taking bonus cards to increase the work value of a work.  Or have more profession/recruiting cards in your hand.  I love it!  This game will get many more plays now!

RATING: 7
Princes of Florence is a very abstract, strategic game that mainly focusses on you and your tableau. There is an element of luck of the draw, and of course your opponents can have a large influence on the available item in the auction, so you'll have to be flexible. When that information is available, you can calculate a lot in this game. With experienced gamers this game can get very mathematical. The artwork of the latest QWG edition is very attractive, and the theme works for the game, although it isn't very attractive for new players. The beauty is in the efficiency engine.
UPDATE: I've decided to sell this game. It doesn't get played enough in my group, and some friends also own a copy. It hurts to part with this game, but it's definately for the best.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
15+  plays.

A game that has grown on me. This game is a classic, though I think it shows its age: compared to the best of the more recent Euro games, it plays a bit slow, and the theme is a bit stale. Down time can be long, and I think the play is too mathematically driven (if you had the resources, you could calculate the best play at any instant). Puerto Rico, while a very different game, is, I think, a good example of a game that came out of the next stage in the evolution of German game design. If you are new to Euro games, hold off on this one.

Update: this game is superb. Not as friendly as some of the others, however, as I've noted, which is why it only get's a rating of 8.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Princes of Florence was the last of the Alea/HiG Euro classics (Puerto Rico et al) from the turn of the century I got to play. 11 years later it still holds up with the new releases although it has a distinctive strategic old-school feel which is part of its charm. It reminded me of Louis XIV (which it predates) in its aim to complete works/missions in most turns. While Louis is the majority take on fulfilment services, PoF is auction-based. Every gamers' game can benefit from some auction and with PoF I'm glad to have a rock solid exponent in my Alea collection. Too bad, that it's the Pro Ludo second German edition which has inferior less functional artwork. It likely won't climb higher than a 7 as it's too strategic to fall within my more tactically focused comfort zone.

RATING: 7

**********************************************

The theme is very strong and the building/development aspect is compelling. However the game plays like a puzzle for me, and a fairly simple one at that. You only have 7 auctions acquisitions and 14 actions over the course of the entire game; how hard can it it be to figure out the optimal course of action? The counter-argument is that those 7 auctions are the core of the game and will make or break your run at the title. The problem is that by the second turn the players are headed down their own paths and there often isn't a whole lot of competition for the things you want. The drama level is surprisingly low. Don't get me wrong, this is a nice little game, but it gets my prize for most overrated game on the 'geek.

RATING: 8
Absolutely amazing game. The auction mechanics often leads to many interesting decisions and change of plans (ie: bluffing to raise the bid but yet unintentionally winning it and having to change your plans entirely). Turn order during auction is also something to take note when making decisions. Bluffing is possible especially when in control of deciding what to bid during the auction to clear off other potential competitors from the real intended item.

The action phase seems to be a bit more streamlined early game. I've found that taking work cards early on seems to be a standard play for the game. Despite of that, players will still have to make interesting and often hard decisions if they want to be above the rest in prestige points.

Plays better with more players.

Fürsten von Florenz

RATING: 8.5
Rio Grande. 2000. $50 (2010)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Essentially, this game is all about managing limited resources: limited money to bid on auctions and purchase actions, limited actions per turn (2), the limited availability of particular resources (buildings, freedoms, etc.), the limited amount of space available in your palazzo, and even the limited number of turns over which the game is played (7).  There is player interaction via auctions and competing to complete the best "work", and difficult choices abound in every area of the game.  It is intense and there is a learning curve, but the game is terribly satisfying and lots of fun!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Not what I was expecting.  Definitely nothing for first timers... there is so much going on, you are likely to lose them.  Many many things going on, but just how much strategy is there?

The auction round really complicates one's ability to do any planning.  Your game outcome is completely dependent on what resources you can win.  Also, there is a lot of luck when pulling cards.

It is a fun game, once you know what you are doing.  Sadly, it didn't scratch that itch for me.

RATING: 5
The game play mechanism is interesting but the overall game is kind of boring. All the profession cards give you the same amount of points. So basically it does not matter, which profession name card you are holding in your hand. The only difference with these cards is that they need different things for scoring (lake for this one, park for the other). If you finally score you get the same amount of points for the lawyer (jurist) as you would get for the philosopher profession card.Also the only interaction between the players is in the auction phase. After that there is basically no possibility to interact or influence the other players. So overall I am kind of disappointed with this game. In my opinion this is just a solitary-playing game.

RATING: 2
I traded this away after one play.  Some of the ideas are okay (like the Tetris buildings and the various Professions), but this game really IS multiplayer solitaire.  Seven rather limited auctions are the extent of your interaction with other players in this game. The rest of the game was just extremely dull downtime.  I can see how certain kinds of people would find this game attractive, but this is the opposite of what I like in a game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I love this game, and can't get enough of it.  While I understand the complaints of 'multi-player solitaire' I don't think that they are accurate, and the fact that a single bad or great auction can dramatically change the course of your strategy should keep you engaged anyway.

I also like that this is a serious multi-player strategy game that doesn't suffer from 'screw the leader,' 'runaway leader,' or 'kingmaker' in any serious form.  Occasionally a dumb move in the auction can cause one of the latter two, but it is rare, and not absolute.

The only possible downside, IMO, is the downtime, which can be made worse by the odd case of analysis paralysis.  The ability to consider your own strategy in this downtime is just as high, though, so it shouldn't be too great a problem for anyone who 'gets' the game.

UPDATE: Well, I finally decided to give this a 10.  The only thing that had been holding me back before was the apparent weakness of the 3-player game, but I've now played many many three-player games with experienced players, and I've seen that it can be at least as tense and interesting as an average 4-player game.  10 it is.

RATING: N/A
NEW EDITION?

Super highly rated game BUT can have a bit of downtime. Requires ability to think ahead (very limited resources and choices, but an abundance of opportunities and choices). 2 player variant plays much quicker than the typical 3-5 player version. Not difficult to learn but definitely NOT an entry level game or a game for newcomers (as it will require a few plays (3-5) before becoming an enjoyable jaunt. Typically sells for $25 online.

NonGamers DO seem to enjoy this. Yet, others have said:
"As always, there are things not to love about the game. Its not a game I'd choose to teach to non-gamers, even though I know that they will love the game after a few plays. Why is this? It probably comes down to how often you can game with a particular non-gaming-group of people. If this is the one shot to get them into gaming, PoF is a big risk. If you're bound to game again in the future, PoF will almost certainly be a succes. Multiple games is key here, I think."
AND
"Non-gamers HATE this game. Period. Playing Princes of Florence with true non-gamers (e.g. folks who played Monopoly yonks ago and vaguely remember trying to learn Risk that one time in college) is akin to trying to entertain your four-year old nephew with the Director's Cut of "Remains of the Day"."
AND (in reply to the previous quote): 
This has not been my experience at all. The rules are simple and the theme/goal of making cool stuff is rather intriguing to people who don't game. New gamers probably won't grasp the strategy or understand how to thread all their actions together throughout the game, but bidding and building is fun in and of itself and very accessible.

REPRINT expect 12/2010 ($30)

Notwithstanding a wonderful ranking, it appears as though there should not be a big rush to get this game. Especially in light of the more FUN games that are likely to get played a lot more.

CONCLUSION: A very big WANT in terms of personally playing it. A very unenthusiastic LIKE in terms of trying it with my students (especially considering all the other games laying dormant).

RPG Rates this as a 1-2 hours playtime and min of 3 players. Until I have a few plays and can determine suitability for the games library, keep it on the Wishlist as a "3"

RATING: 8.5
+
very good strategical play possible 
1)with the prestige cards
2) which rounds will you make a work - which rounds will you use to get the most advantagous cards ? careful planning is needed.
original system : you choose if you convert your work points into victory points or money
good bidding system gives enough interaction - sometimes painful decision
very little luck
unforgiving game ; don't run out on money - but this can easily be prevented. Try to have on the end of the round at least 800 florins (exception :going to the last round) 
contrary as many say ; there is sufficient interaction in the bidding ; bluff plays also a considerable role
-
 downtime can be a problem with AP gamers ; but you always can optimalise your own plan
 after many plays there's a certain danger of scripted play. But you can enhance the variability using the expansion, which makes the game more forgiving.

RATING: 7.25
Played three times and enjoyed it more each time.  The real challenge in the auction phase comes in determining whether you can jack up someone else's expense by bidding but not get stuck winning something you didn't want, and whether to start the bidding with something you don't want just to get another player to take it and be out of the running for the rest of the auction.  I like that drawing a card actually means drawing five and choosing the best--you might not get a game-winning mega card, but you'll almost always get something you can use.

RATING: 4
Auctions, scarce turns which demands on a careful plan, limitation on resources, puzzle-like element, beautiful artwrks, interesting theme combined in a 1.5-hour game... Good, but not for me.

Firstly, it is too analyzable. You can count remaining actions, remaining auctions, remaining cards, also points you can get. You can decide all that which you'll buy and which you'll do after one or two rounds, sometimes before the game begins. (Well, you don't have to decide in advance, if you don't mind a loss)

This almost perfect analyzability deprives this game of much fun. Keeping track of all that which I should do and which I can do is exhausting. Fail on getting required resource is frustrating. Waiting for my turn watching other player actions which is not significant for me after deciding what I'll do is boring.

Secondly(this is related to first reason), it is very solitary. All the players' heart is filled with their own boards, their own current status and their future actions. Each players' actions rarely arouse interest of other players. Even the auctions becomes procedural with experienced players.

First and second reason combined: there rarely are astonishing actions, fulfilling moments, funny situations, only methodological selection.

This would be a good game for a planner, analyzer, optimizer... which I am not.

Florence, ville des arts !
En tant que prince de la Renaissance, vous ne désirez plus satisfaire votre soif de reconnaissance et de pouvoir par la force politique ou militaire.
Les temps ont biens changés, les arts et la culture fabriquent aujourd’hui une réputation, plus que la force des armes ni la richesse.
Pour rivaliser de connaissances et de raffinements, vous allez devoir permettre aux créateurs de réunir tous les ingrédients suffisants pour qu’une œuvre sublime surgisse au monde.
Les muses ne suffisent plus ! Les princes de Florence sont là pour le mécénat.
Et puis qui sait ? Votre nom aussi pourrait passer à la postérité !

RATING: 6
Princes of Florence is one of the drier Euro's.  I suppose in the end that was the biggest reason I didn't like the game as much, but there were others.  The potential imbalance in player ability is not as severe as Puerto Rico, but knowing how much the auctioned items are worth is a large part of the game.  Another small distaste was the way the progressive scores are affected by lucky draws to the profession deck.  In a game of optimization, you could easily draw or not (yes, even when picking up multiple cards) a profession that is a near perfect fit while your opponents do the opposite.

To me, this game is more about passive conflict in that you are attempting to make your opponents overpay for their profession tools almost as much as you are attempting to complete your own works.  I am beginning to see that I do not generally like those types of games.

RATING: 9
Princes has been one of my favorite games for a long time.  I love the auctioning aspect to the game, along with the many minor competitions throughout (fighting over the freedoms, getting the right bonus card to edge out your opponents for the largest work, etc.), but that you still maintain and improve your own city in the best way that you can.  I really love playing this game, although the dominance of the Jester strategy has taken away some fun in recent games.  However, I always feel that I've done quite well even if I end up being dead last, which greatly improves my overall enjoyment of the game.  Definitely a heavier game with long-term planning, but certainly one that I will not turn down the chance to play.

RATING: 7
Game in a Nutshell: Auction and buy the pieces needed to create great works. The better the work, the more points gained towards victory or more money made to put into future works... your choice.

Played as: 3 and 5 player

Pros: Finally, after four plays of this game, I just played using the right rules and I feel sooo much better about it. Very balanced (too balanced?) gameplay makes game close and interesting beginning to end.

Cons: Even though I've only played this once- the right way, with players still working to grasp the new (correct) flow of the game, I feel in the long run this will be a game of splitting hairs. It's like something as simple as overbidding 100 florin in one auction could make all the difference rather then implementing sound decisions the entire game in the action phase.

Other: Negative assumptions about this game being decided solely in the auction phase aside, I still think the action phase is what makes this game as fun as it is. Simply put, you may win the game in the auctions, but play and enjoy because of the actions.

Best Use: Playing with your regular group of gamers on game night.

RATING: 6
After a couple plays, I think I've figured this game out well enough to know it's not for me. At least I was able to win one against a group of serious gamers (though not to my knowledge PoF aces).

It strikes me that it is a "calculating" auction game. You can calculate approximately what something will be worth to you for the whole game, and bid approximately that.

For an auction game, I prefer Ra, which to me is more of an intuitive auction game. You can't bid exactly what something is worth to you. For one you don't know, because there is significant randomness in what will come up later that will affect the value. Also, you can't bid what something is worth, because your bid is not in the same currency as the value. Suns have no direct relationship to points, unlike in Princes of Florence where money has a fixed conversion ratio to VPs, although you can only apply the conversion at a specific time and in limited amounts.

RATING: 8
Enjoyed the auction dynamics as well as the lack of direct competition.  It was fun to try to plan future developments, though the indirect competition for resources and auction goods was challenging (in a good way) to incorporate into a plan.  The game requires significant amounts of planning in the first few moves to be successful.  I definitely want to play many more times, but need to think about it beforehand.

The game has a huge element of experience in it; I typically play with folks who LOVE the game, and who have their strategies already planned out on the first move (and who know the actual value of every auctionable-item).  This makes the game difficult to play for me, as I don't know this (and thus make copious mistakes).

RATING: 6
Solid game that felt a lot like Agricola but with auctions instead of worker placement. Both present players with many options, considerable constraints, and a nifty little tableau you're trying to build out for maximum points. I do find worker placement a more elegant mechanism for handling action/resource selection, but I can see the potential here for more varied strategies. I also like the relatively swift playing time, but that's mitigated by the need for five (or maybe four) players.

RATING: 7.5
A good auction game that stands the test of time. The auctions are meaningful and tense.  The choices of how to build your principality are deep and multifaceted. Each game led to a lot of discussions about the tactical and strategic choices we made.  This is a game that can stick with you.

I found the art and graphic design to be dry and dated.  I think that in this day of beautifully painted boards and high detail minis, it's hard for a game like this to attract players.  Nevertheless, I've really enjoyed every play!

RATING: 4
Way too much downtime between player turns. The auctions, which are the only real player interaction in the game, are too short and mechanical. Overall its a game with a lot of silence. A good example of the "elegant" Eurogames format taken too far with the end result being as soulless number crunching exercise. This game is my litmus test for player compatability - if someone tells me they really like playing this game a lot then I'm unlikely to enjoy playing anything with them. My advice is if you want a "multiplayer solitaire" eurogame then stick with Puerto Rico because it has a LOT less downtime and MUCH more player interaction than PoF.

RATING: 8
A good Euro game that for some is a little dry. There is little room for luck here as planning and execution are rewarded and for this reason it can be a game that favours the more experienced.

The theme here is that each player is a Prince and they are trying to display their greatness by supporting the most thriving province of skilled artisans and intellects. Providing for these persons will hopefully pay off in great works that you can then sell for a profit and thus enhance your reputation.

Definitely a game that relies on your personal taste so I recommend trying it if you haven't played it before.

RATING: 9.7
A hodgepodge of Euro mechanics combine into one of the most interesting game designs in all of gaming. The game greatly rewards strategy, even though you only get about 14 actions over the course of a game. Despite a relatively simple ruleset, there are myriad paths to victory. The auctions are derided by some, but they enable the game to remain highly variable from play to play. A classic.

RATING: 8

You become the Patron of many professionals, artists, and scholars.  You attempt for the right fit to build the better works of art. You steal professionals from your rivals.  And finally, through competition the works are elevated with each new turn. In 2000, before Puerto Rico and Caylus, this was the game.  Dated, but like the Renaissance itself, timeless and seminal.

RATING: 6
Great game that at its core is an auction game.  After the auction phase everyone gets their turn to perform actions that get you buildings, freedoms, and a few other things that make your profession cards worth more when you play them.  The fact that there are MANY different paths to victory makes this game re-playable and enjoyable.  

Although I have only played once I can already say that this is a deep game that will have to be played a few times before you deeply understand the strategies and pathways to victory.  Tough for new players to compete because the game is based around bidding and auctions that new players will not know what to go for.

RATING: 10
Take the jesters if you can. (See the less viable builder strategy at Fawkes)
The only player interaction in the game are the auctions, which will decide the eventual winner, so you have to pay attention there. Because of the importance of the auction and in spite of what its critics say, this game is no solitaire.  Plays within an hour and a half, and it is great with 4 and fantastic with 5.
Highest rated game on BGG from the year 2000.
The Princess and the Muse expansion changes the values of the auctioned items and hence the strategies . It offers a more varied game in a longer time frame. The base game is tighter, less forgiving of mistakes. The expansion is still a 10 though.
In the Cooperative builders expansion you are dependent upon your neighbors' cooperation in getting VPs or money, and have limited control over that. You have zero control over the negotiations of non neighbors. I was not impressed by my only play so far, and may not be enthusiastic to give it another try.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4.5
A strange breed of game. I quite like the auction and the tile laying aspect and the general feel of the game. Creating a work reminds me of character driven ccgs - such as Babylon 5 or Vampire. You have your character; John Sheridan, Astrid the Vampire or in the case POF a Poet or Cartographer. Then tool them up with a PPG Rifle, a chainsaw or a nice forest and library. Whack a couple of events or bonuses on them and send them to support a diplomacy conflict, inflict a bleed or create a lovely piece of art. The only difference is that in the ccgs the opponents can oppose or disrupt you plans whereas in POF you create the art and just score the points.

The choice between victory points or more money is nice, but the final outcome is really flat, especially as you plan the whole game for a couple of big payouts at the end. (6.5)

RATING: 8.5
Probabilmente il miglior gioco di aste in circolazione. Il poco margine di scelta sulle innumerevoli opportunità fornite ad ogni turno (solo sette) impongono aste agguerrite specie ad inizio gioco. La nuova versione di Principi di Firenze prevede un'ulteriore asta (fase A2) intermedia per aggiudicarsi anche un favore. L'esperienza è importante in questo gioco perché ogni asta risulterà determinante soprattutto se equiparata al turno in cui viene fatta. Favori reali, costruttori e joker andranno a ruba ma ricordatevi che si vince con i punti vittoria e quindi anche le carte Prestigio sono da tenere in considerazione. Un gioco molto bello da giocare assolutamente in almeno 4 o 5, da non proporre in 3 quando le aste diventano poco avvincenti. Trovo la confezione e i materiali della versione italiana molto eleganti, ma il carattere non del tutto leggibile. Un gioco consigliatissimo!

RATING: 10
A glorious and very stylish gaming experience. The combination of auction and tile-placement feel made for each other, and the thematics are poetic, to say the least. Art and general design are beautiful if almost entirely inaccessible, but the subtle up-and-down dynamic to the interaction and construction elements is perfectly pitched. Potentially agonising downtime is an irritant, and the game is surely best when you've had the opportunity to learn the decks of cards well.

Further Thoughts: A couple of outstandingly good plays have pushed this one up to a 9.5. There really is very little to match it, one would think.

[2010/01/27]

Slick game, and i mean totally slick...

Our gaming group pulled this out as a "staple game"... and the first playwas like most learning games, but i instantly realized that i wanted to  play it again... so low and behold, My wife and I got a second go at this game, with our gaming group... I got a semi lucky draw to start off with, and i played  with a good strategy... this game held my interest, and it was very competitive... at the end of the game My wife won with a super score (beating me by 1!)... and it just so happened, that my final move to give myself an extra 3 points, took 8 points (and the victory) away from another player... SUPER CLOSE GAME...

To reiterate, this was our second play... we picked it up easily, and it has proven to be totally fun.... also it is an intuitve game, thats why my wife and i could just pick it up and be competitive with veteran Florence players 

This game is further proving to me that Auctions can make games awesome... the auctions here change the ideal way to play each game. In addition to good strategy, you need to know how to evaluate value at every turn.

I recomend this HIGHLY to anyone that likes strategy game with multiple ways to win.

I bet that learning this game by yourself would be rather hard... but it's worth it

RATING: N/A
The first time I played this game I was really disappointed with it. The reason was we were playing it with 2-players and we had to play with 2 boards each. But, now that we have the 2-player rules from the Alea Treasure Box expansion, we can enjoy the game again...for the first time.

Now, with the 2-player rules, this is a great medium weight game that can be played in a fairly short amount of time. It's meaty and clever and fun. The betting in the auction is still a little off...even with the 2-player rules. But, the game play is not affected too much. I can see where this game probably shines best with more players. 

The artwork and font used in this game is annoying! The neutral light tan and brown color of everything in the game does not make this game appealing to anyone in our game groups. Therefore, this game only comes out for my wife and I. I fear never really getting to play this game with more players because of it's overall drab appearance and game theme appeal. I really wish this game would be picked up by a publisher and the artwork updated.

The Alea Treasure Box expansion adds 2-player rules for this game and it has an additional expansion of 6 new characters to choose from in a second auction. You bid in the normal auction first, followed by a second auction on the characters (even with 2-players, this second auction works very well). The new characters provide some powerful new options for everyone to choose from. They are maybe too powerful for a 2-player game...but I can see it adding some nice options for playing with up to 5 players.

RATING: 7
規則:
*每位玩家拿取一個競標標誌物跟一個計分棋子，四張選三張職業卡，3500元
*森林 湖泊 公園 弄臣 建築師 聲望卡 招募卡(開著) 放一邊
*所有建築物 自由(個數等於玩家-1) 職業卡 獎勵卡放另外一邊
*遊戲進行七輪，每輪順時鐘換起始玩家，每輪兩個階段
1.拍賣階段 每人買第一邊的東西一個
起始玩家開始選擇一個物品競標起始價固定200，加價固定100或pass標到的物品鎖定此回合不能再標，最後一個付200拿，也可不拿
*景觀類第二個以後每個三分並為特別職業作品加分，弄臣作品加兩分，聲望卡遊戲最後加分，招募卡可招募對手使用過的職業到手上，該召募卡取代原本的位置，作品都會加分
*遊戲中獲得蓋著的卡片都是抽五張選一張，剩下四張放牌庫底
2.行動階段 起始玩家開始 分別執行兩個行動在換人(可不做，不能在行動中亂序)
A 完成作品 使用職業卡完成作品要超過回合低標，將作品分數用棋子標是在圖版上，每點作品分擲100元，此時可每200轉成一分(只能用該作品獲得的錢)，該輪作品分最高的獲得三分(一回合完成兩個作品，作品分是取高的不是取和)
B 建造建築物 沒建築師700，兩個建築師300，第三個之後免費，每間三分
*放置建築物除非有兩個以上建築師才能相鄰，否則不行，景觀無限制，每種建築只能造一次，不能移動拆除
C 拿職業卡(一次) 付300
D 拿自由(一次) 付300，不能重複
F 拿獎勵卡 付300
*行動結束後 作品分數最高+3分 平手都加
*遊戲中可用每一分換100元
*****兩人規則
*起始2500，每種自由一個 在抽隨機兩個共五個
*拍賣起標300 景觀一種被買另外兩種一起鎖
*聲望卡平手獲得()內分數
****擴充
*加六張特殊角色卡片， 銀行加上面放600
*不能用分數換錢
*多一個特殊角色拍賣階段
*競標放在該卡片的一個數字上，後來的必須放更高並把超過的棋子還給該玩家，價格為該數字乘100
*在下次特殊角色競標前要使用腳色卡
*銀行家沒被買上面在+100

RATING: 5.5
Tetris with cardboard pieces in Renaissance Italy. Everyone was raving about this game: "classic, elegant, blah blah blah." And maybe this is not the game's fault but I was underwhelmed when I played this game. And, by all accounts, I should love this game because it has everything I love about a good, solid Euro: historical, simple in strategy but has depth, beautiful components, a nasty auction, finicky little pieces that you can step on and hurt yourself...everything! But Princes of Florence somehow left me wanting more. It didn't feel thematic nor atmospheric. It just felt sort of...dull. Don't get me wrong: this game is harmless. RATING: 9
50+ games. One of the best auction games out there. It really shines when played amongst experienced players, where the valuations are broadly known and therefore tightly contested - how far over general value consensus can you afford to go without throwing away the game; given what other people have done so far, what's likely to go for dear and cheap next round. There's exactly the right balance between actions and auctions to keep the game flowing rather than being repetitive.

RATING: 6.5
TRY Aug/05 (BSW, as die Fursten von Florenz):...still learning... tentative rating.  I find this one tough to rate accurately.

While the game is interesting, and has lots going on, some of the later auctions become fairly game-breaking, as some people won't get much/anything useful.  After 3 plays, I understand WHAT is going on, but I'm still not sure just how much FUN it is.  It's got me thinking about strategy, though.

And basic game concept, which, as one of the Princes of Florence, is essentially to 'hire' some artists, make them as comfortable as possible based on their preferences (and common ones, like jesters & other artists), and then choose when to make them display the 'art' that they've produced for you (and then release them from your service).

I find most auction games to be tough on beginners (while they learn the relative values); this is no exception.

As well, the concept of an artist's 'work value' is abstract, as it is just a temporary result then converted into Florins ($) and/or Prestige Points (PP, or victory points).  You need a cheat-sheet handy for calculating these WVs, just to figure out how to affect it with all the available influences (buildings in 3 sizes, builders, landscapes, recruiters, jesters, bonus cards, prestige cards, freedoms), and the increasing round WV minimums.  Whew!  That's a LOT to keep straight!

All of this affects its approachability, to me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a game of planning. You play 7 rounds, and in each round you do an auction for powerful tiles and cards, and then an action phase where each player gets 2 actions. One of your action options is to build a building, which has to fit on your little tetris style board. Another option is to score a card, which can give you credits that you either take in any combination of money or in actual points at a rate of 2 credits for 1 point. In order to score a card, it must be worth at least the min value for that round, which goes up every turn. The cards score more depending on how many things and what things you've built. This all works out to be a very tight and tense game of budgeting and planning. It's a delightful thinky old euro that desperately needs a face lift. I bought a copy of the Italian Edition that has nicer art, so my copy had a strange hodge podge of that and the English version, due to the game having language dependent cards.

:nostar: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:star: Handles 5 or more players
:star: Good with different player counts
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:star: Re-playability
:star::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The theme in PoF, based on renaissance Italy, is not necessarily something that appeals to everyone. And while there is plenty of conflict in the game thanks to limited resources and an auction mechanism to get those resources, the conflict is also indirect.

You aim to create a palace and its associated landscapes, buildings etc. such that you maximize the "work value" of tasks completed by various illustrious professionals such as poets, artists etc. The game requires thinking ahead in terms of hand management to not just complete tasks in the current round, but also in future rounds.

Although it can play upto 5, I think the sweet spot is 3 because there is enough in-game text that can lead to some downtime between turns. (This may be somewhat lessened if all players are very familiar with every available card and how it can be best used.)

RATING: N/A
Yet another Previously Owned Euro. It wasn't terrible, it flowed smoothly (it's Kramer; what do you expect?), and it featured things I normally like: auctions, polyominoes, simple rules, and clear goals. Strategic Euros simply stopped getting played, and there's no way I wanted to hang on to something that only saw action once every two years. 

Despite it being "the game that ruined Eurogames" or whatever Michael Barnes called it, I don't think it's that objectionable. It's even enjoyable, if a bit dry. But I don't think it's a fair representation of the sorts of dry, convoluted, solitaireish games that dominated Eurogaming for a while. I think games by Stefan Feld and Vital Lacerda should get more blame (or credit?) for turning Euros into overcomplicated, solitaire, tactical point grabs. Princes of Florence is driven by player interaction, is simple, and moves very cleanly. It's honestly not that bad.

RATING: 6
Based on 1 play and observing others, and reading about it a lot - I'm shocked this game is rated #7. It is a flawed game that has more luck than people are admitting. Among equal players, the winner is likely whoever gets the Professions that work the best together. End of story. 

Add in the [b]facts[/b] that it's a "rich get richer" game (where the leader will be getting the most money, which allows him to outbid people) and that it is simply not designed for 5 players (there simply are not enough Professions to work - they get used, and then the last 3 rounds it's all about the Recruiter auction). It shows me that the main reason this game is so well regarded is the group-think of BGG : it came out in 2000, and was played a lot in early BGG, well-rated at the time, and that influenced years of followers to play it, and rate it high so they could be agreeing with the enlightened BGG crowd.

RATING: 8.5
I love Princes of Florence.  So many ways to try and win, so little time.  One of the reasons I like this game is that I can't find the "best" way to play it.  Sometimes I go for more jesters, sometimes for more builders, sometimes for bonus cards, etc.  They all seem viable - I just keep thinking if I play one more game I can figure it out.  Yet, I can't, and that is what keeps bringing me back.  

The auction is fun, trying to fake folks into thinking you want one item when you really want another.  I also like the building placing aspect.  I always enjoy games where I "build" something - whether it be a city, or an empire, etc.  The layout of my villa gives me that feeling.

RATING: 8
--Dec2003-- Very cool: too many things to do, in too little time.

--Sept2004-- Played this again after too long without.  Much tighter game than I remembered - probably a reflection of the players this time.  Very nice.

--Aug2005-- My dad's review: "Peaceful and fun."  Our game wasn't particularly competitive, yet he wanted to play again the next day...

On reflection, this game is one of those delightful ones where complete newbies and I can have a completely fun time attempting some suboptimal and useless strategies.  It seems to me that even in the face of wildly differing abilities, the poorer players will still enjoy the experience - rather unlike the _Puerto Rico_ or _Euphrat & Tigris_ experience.  A really nice design.

--Dec2007-- Bumped down to 8 due to lack of play.

RATING: 10
I love PoF. Is there alot of player interaction? No. Is it a great game? Yes. You only have 7 chances to bid and thus 7 chances to make your game and ruin your opponents. I think the game really needs 5 players to shine as this increases competition for everything, but with 5 the game is one of the best out there.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This game exemplifies the "Euro-style", which to me means it has a lot of obstacles to me liking it: there's a lot of "playing solitaire against each other", no direct conflict, and the whole game you execute some strategy and you have no feedback on whether you are doing well until the game is over and it's too late to do anything about it. I never felt like I really had a handle on what was going on and was just doing one thing after another. Also, the rule where the amount of money each player has is secret - even though the starting amount, and all revenues and expenditures are public - totally blows my mind as to how pointless it is, since it means that in order to be playing properly, each player should bring a pen and paper and track everyone else's money anyways. 

In general, Princes struck me as very similar to Puerto Rico in every regard, so if you like that game, you will probably like this one. 

I think this game would actually be really interesting if each player was writing their own AI to play against each other, but actually playing it yourself is much less enjoyable.

RATING: 8
Well built Euro, which has an uncommon focus on STRATEGY rather than TACTICS. For that alone, this game has a place in my collection. You need to have a plan from turn one, and then adjust to your opponents, but you must stick with your plan. Clever use of cards means that it's unlikely everyone will be gunning for the same stuff - limiting unintentional screwage - which is a nice touch.

However, this does require everyone to know the game to roughly the same degree, and mean that you can be punished for early mistakes throughout the game. There are no catch-up mechanics.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
For me this is the best of the Euro games, hands down. It's an excellent low-luck planning game, with some clever mechanics to keep the pace accelerating (increased return on builders, increasing minimum WV, prestige card goals etc). Deserves its place in the top five. If you like Puerto Rico or San Juan, you'll enjoy PoF (as it has a similar feel, but is different enough to play in the same evening as one of the above). It is quite unforgiving for new players, though.

I find PoF to be more interactive than it's given credit for, especially in the auction and recruiting mechanisms (and both of these are better with more players). The many different paths to victory should give it a long lifespan. 

The more I play it the more I like it. Best with five players, but quite playable with four. The 3P game lacks the tension of the 5P, but even so it's still interactive.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
I thought there might be a problem with the replay value of this game. I was wrong. I'm really enjoying each outing of this - trying different strategies and tweaking favourites. When other players are having their turns I'm usually thinking, so downtime is no problem for me. It's like half a game and half a puzzle that moves with actions the other players take. Experienced players polish it off in an hour, including setup and packing away time. Auction values can take a couple of games to get about right, and sometimes those have to be thrown out the window anyway less you allow a player to get away with dominating Jesters. I could highly recommend this to gamers who don't want too much confrontation in their games. It's very balanced and this keeps gamers coming back for more. Outstanding.

RATING: 10
This game is nothing short of brilliant, and the key to it is the auction.  If you cant get what you need at a reasonable price or if you're not flexible enough to be able to bow out of an auction or two, you'll struggle.  And why are there always two people fighting over something (usually something pretty benign like a park) in round 7?  Yes, managing those seven auctions is key.  Because of this, I think there's a lot more player interaction than most people give the game credit for.  There's multiple paths to victory, and the interplay among the different items you can buy and the scoring is just so-well thought out.  Brilliant!

RATING: 2.5
Staring into the abyss of "the game that ruined eurogames". Yup it is this one. Definitely. It's not as bad as a game, but bringing to mind all of its offspring and the mainstream euro trend it sends shivers down my spine. 

And it's not elegant, no it still has the quirkiness of the German games of old. The puzzle part is far less straightforward than in Goa for instance, the money or points decision is very gamey and old school. There's a lot of juggling around rough edges trying to satisfy threshold for scoring which increases each round. And it's this quirkiness that give it charm - the spacial element, the very stylised graphic style, there's even some logic behind profession and it's demands (well sometimes). 

The jury's still out (only played solo, I have to see the downtime problem, which probably balances with all the AP the juggling produces). 
It's educational. Happy not to own it.

RATING: 8
Fairly dense auction game where players bid on items worth points towards completing 'works'. The base costs for completing works gets harder each round. Toss in a tetri minigame of arranging pieces on a gridded board and some special effects cards and you get PoF.

As with auction games, experience rewards players. The game looks complex but is deceptively simple and very tightly developed. One of Kramer's best middleweight Euros.

RATING: 9
An average game at 3 players, great at 4 players and awesome at 5 players. If you are able to get a 4-5p group then this is an amazing auction game. It is extremely tense -- do you put up what you want for auction, or do you put up something else hoping to pick up what you really want for cheap? Do you pay lots of money for that thing you want, or do you try to pick up cheap items? Do you take money or VPs? Do you enter the auction just to drive the price up, but risking that you might be stuck with the item?

You have only 7 rounds, which means you can only get seven items from the auction pool through the whole game. Getting any one means foregoing something else and because its an auction you have to deal with the bidding of every other player. So getting what you want is not guaranteed at all. When do you drop out of the auction and pick up something else? And how much impact does that have on your strategy?

Tough questions, tough decisions and tense bidding. Amazing game.

One thing: Don't bother with the Princess and Muse expansion that is also in the box. It just makes an elegant game unnecessarily complicated.

RATING: 6
[2011-07-16]
Talán megint abba a pechbe futott bele a játék, mint sok más öreg az én esetemben, azaz, hogy sokára próbáltam kis a megjelenése után. De sajnos azt kell mondanom több dolog nem tetszett a játékban, mint ami igen. A játékosok saját udvarukat építik, épületeket, erdőket, tavakat, parkokat hoznak létre. Építőmestereket, udvari bolondokat szerződtetnek és szabadságjogokat szereznek. Ezek mind-mind arra szolgálnak, hogy egy-egy művész különböző műveket hozzon létre az udvarunkban, ezzel győzelmi pontot hozva nekünk. Teljesen jól hangzó dolog, mégis nem igazán győzött meg. Kezdjük a jó dolgokkal. Nagyon kellemesen küszöböli ki a játék a szerencse szerepét. A mindig öt lapból való húzás miatt egyszer sem éreztem a játék közben, hogy nagy pechem lett volna, mert nem jutottam jó laphoz, pedig fizettem érte. A játékban kicsi a konfrontáció mértéke, bátran ajánlható akár családi játéknak is, nem is éreztem annyira nehéznek. Egyedül a pénzre kell figyelni, de jó, hogy pontokból pénzt is lehet vásárolni.
Jöjjenek azonban a kevésbé tetsző dolgok. A játék első kiadásainak kinézete nem túl szép, nagyon sok a szöveg, ami simán megoldható lett volna ikonokkal, így nehéz is az elején tájékozódni, főleg az épületek tekintetében. Fix 7 körös a játék, ami jól hangzik elsőre, de ahogy haladunk előre egyre nagyobb a holtidő a játékban és érdekes módon az éppen korábban dicsért laphúzás mechanizmusa miatt. Mivel mindig 5 lapból választunk, három esetben is, ahol tényleg jól meg kell fontolni mit vesz el a játékos sokat kell várni egymásra, főleg ha valaki két laphúzó akciót hajt végre a körében. 4 játékossal is sok volt a holtidő, lehet 5-en le se ülnék hozzá, pedig elvileg úgy a legjobb a játék. A 7 kör miatt nem mindenki ugyanannyiszor kezdőjátékos, ami elsőre nem tűnik nagy előnynek, de azért van jelentősége. Ilyen most megjelenő játékokban már ritkán fordulhatna elő. Az aukció érdekes eleme lehetne a játéknak, de úgy érzem mivel minden dolog használható valamire, sőt tulajdonképpen mind hoz győzelmi pontot is nincs nagyon értelme, maximum a végén lévő körökben nagyon erősködni az aukció alatt. Simán alapáron el kell vinni, ami marad. Talán ez a rész lehet, ami öten izgalmasabb, bár öt játékos esetén is úgy érzem ami marad, az is jó lehet a játékban. További problémám a játékban levő stratégia lehetőségek száma. Én úgy érzem egyértelműen a legjobb mindenből kicsit elvet követni, bár lehetnek ettől eltérések valahogy nem érzem többutasnak. Ezt persze illene több játék után megítélni, de valahogy úgy érzem nincs túl sok lehetőség a játékban a sok épület és aukció alatt szerezhető dolgok ellenére sem.
Egy nagyon magasan jegyzett játék a Firenze hercegei, nekem mégis csalódás volt. Biztos belejátszott a játék puritán kinézete és a sok holtidő, illetve, hogy a játékot nem a megjelenésekor tudtam kipróbálni, de valahogy nem érzem például annyira időtállónak, mint mondjuk az általam szintén nem annyira magasztosított El Grandét, de annál valahogy éreztem miért annyira népszerű, ennél egyáltalán nem. Ettől függetlenül nem szeretnék lebeszélni róla senkit, mert nem rossz játékról van szó egyáltalán.

RATING: 4
I can see why people like this methodical and clinical game, but it's not really for me.  Very little interaction (you play your board like solitare) except for bidding.  But even in the bidding, you just bid for what you need and sometimes you get lucky and get stuff cheap, sometimes you don't. You can only make choices in the auction based on what other people have made on previous turns, but personal goals are just too important to give up in order to counter what your oponents may (or may not) be looking for.  It is just too risky to bid up things you don't need. The real bone, of course, is if you don't manage your money or prestige correctly you get to wait around and do nothing for a long time.  

Worst of all is the downtime.  After you are out of the auction there is some serious sitting and waiting time until your turn comes around.  Because of the way the game is set up, you can't really do simultaneous personal turns (like Goa).  

It was innovative for its time but after Puerto Rico and Goa this game has become obsolete. Also, should win an award for worst font in a game, everything is quite difficult to read.  

Dry and Boring.

RATING: 7
The simplest breakdown of this game is that it is played in a series of 7 rounds with two phases, the first phase is an auction and the second phase is playing Tetris.

In more complete terms this is an optimization game where you acquire different pieces and parts for your player area representing your estate in Florence, trying to earn the most prestige points (victory points) by game end.  The game lasts seven rounds and there are two phases in each round.  The first phase is an auction.  It is a really simple auction where every starting bid is 200 florin and each increment is 100 florin until there is a winner.  There are 7 different items available for auction and only one of each can be won each turn.

The second phase is the action phase.  There are a few different actions you can take here but everything is in the service of constructing a great work.  Each player has profession cards in their hand and by playing one of these cards during the action phase that person constructs a work of art.  The value of this work is determined by the things you have collected at your estate and each round of the game has an increasing requirement for minimum work value.  Also, the total value dictates how much money you will receive for the work, which can also be exchanged for prestige points, and the most valuable work at the end of each round earns a point bonus as well.

I think this game has a decent amount of depth.  There are a lot of different decisions to be made at different points from which items to put up for auction, how much to spend, to even where to place items in the limited space in your estate so you don't block yourself off in the future.  There are multiple opportunities to earn points so you can play differently and refine your game each play.  It also plays at a reasonably fast clip.  There can always be AP to slow the game down but for the most part the turns can go by pretty quickly.

The art design leaves something to be desired for me but I do like the insert (I have the Alea big box series version).  I also think it is reasonably accessible with a thin and straightforward rules set, plus everyone knows the rules to Tetris.  This makes it a great, quick, entry level auction and optimization game that seasoned gamers will enjoy as well for the opportunity to fine tune their game play.

PORTUGUÊS: O jogo começa por ter um tema muito bonito - ser o Príncipe de Florença promovendo cultura. Gosto da produção, a mecânica é muito simples de entender embora seja um jogo difícil de vencer. Muito equilibrado em todas as suas componentes. O leilão e a escassez de alguns recursos valorizam a interactividade entre os jogadores, embora este não seja propriamente um trunfo do jogo. Se tivesse uma maior interactividade seria um jogaço!

Despite the aging PoF still is one of my first picks when thinking about a 5 player game. I really loved the game from the first time I've played it. The theme made things easier to me although in the end this is an abstract game. No matter what I think PoF is one hell of a game, medium weight, good to introduce to newbies but also appreciated by gamers and above all of this is a very elegant game with few but strong and very well combined mechanisms. A must have in any collection.

Played again after several months/years and still like it. A classic. I understand that some of the mechanisms may seem a bit old and dated but I really still enjoy to play it. There are no secrets in the strategy anymore but an occasional play is the solution.

RATING: 10
One of the best games by Kramer, and that is saying something. PoF is playable in 60-90 minutes, but for such a short game, it's surprising strategic. By turn 3 or 4, you should have a clear idea on what you're going to do in the second half of the game, and make sure you have the necessary resources. Many say that it's only really great with 5, but I always enjoy playing this, regardless of the number of players.

RATING: 6
I understand now why Princes of Florence is poster-child/whipping-boy for the worst features of eurogames -- a tense, difficult game with multiple auctions and scarce resources, it is also a dry, themeless work simulator rendered in shades of brown.

Much as I can appreciate the difficult decisions involved, I strongly dislike the core "work value" system -- arbitrary point values for building or purchasing arbitrary sets of stuff -- as well as the various random cards awarding work value or prestige for having different variations of things in those arbitrary sets.

It's a good game but there is nothing in it for me to love and no reason to choose it over other games.

RATING: 8
[5 plays] It is a decent low luck, pretty straight forward game with different strategy options, and an interesting theme.  I really like this plotting, planning, calculating type games.  Figure out how to setup better than the other players and out plan and auction them.  The auction takes a couple plays to figure out the right value for things.  The more I have played this game the tighter the games have become.  The game really does revolve around the auction phase.  The trick is to force people to overpay for the "great" things available each auction, while at the same time getting good deals on the things you need to attract the professions you are trying to lure to your city.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[RGG 2000 Edition] {Medieval, City building, economic; auction, utility card; optimal 4+p} PoF is a gamers' game. It is a multifaceted game - there are multiple paths of victories and no single strategy will forever work. PoF has a good balance - there are no overpower cards and the luck is well controlled. The drawing top-5 card is useful to reduce luck but it's quite artificial. Players have to take care of many things: money, buildings, cards and layout of the principality, which is not easy. It is very unforgiving; thus, it's not suitable for new players.

There is enough player interaction during auctions and competition of resources; however, the interaction is not strong enough to engage all players.

The rule is clear and easy to understand. The theme is unique and works well, which makes PoF intuitive.  Everything integrates with the game nicely except the Bonus cards, which I find them awkward in the game (it seems to be an artificial product to balance the game or to let players have alternate ways to complete a work).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
